	{font-family:Chicago;
	panose-1:2 11 6 4 2 2 2 2 2 4;}

	{font-family:"\@PMingLiU";
	panose-1:2 1 6 1 0 1 1 1 1 1;}
@font-face
	{font-family:"AR CENA";
	panose-1:2 11 6 4 2 2 2 2 2 4;}
@font-face
	{font-family:"Proxima Nova";
	panose-1:2 11 6 4 2 2 2 2 2 4;}

	mso-style-link:Header;}

	mso-style-link:Footer;}

	{mso-style-name:"";
	text-decoration:underline;
	color:teal;}

	{mso-style-name:"";
	text-decoration:line-through;
	color:red;}

	font-family:"Calibri",sans-serif;}

 /* Page Definitions */

<div class="word-content">
<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='text-align:center'><b><span
style='font-size:14.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>MISUSED
BIBLICAL TEXTS</span></b></p>

<p class=MsoNormal align=center style='text-align:center'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Virgil
Warren, PhD </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Comments
below have more to do with interpretation mistakes than with false ideas. Regardless
of the correctness of the beliefs involved, many texts do not indicate them.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Readers
misuse scriptures for several reasons. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(1)
Texts get quoted incorrectly in popular usage. <i>Money is the root of all
evil, </i>for example, misquotes the text, <i>The <u>love</u> of money is the
root of all evil.</i> [<u>incorrect quotation</u>]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(2)
The parts of a text get connected the wrong way<i>. By grace you are saved
through faith, and that </i>[faith]<i> is not of yourselves; it is a gift from
God.</i> <i>That</i> does not refer to faith, but to the salvation indicated
in the verbas the neuter form for that in the Greek text shows. [<u>internal
misconnection</u>]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> (3)
Statements applied more broadly than context warrants, a typical problem with
reading proverbs. <i>Death came by mankind</i> (1 Corinthians </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>15</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:21)
has been used to suppose animals did not die or eat other animals before Adam
sinned. [<u>universalizing specific reference</u>; allegorizing the comparison]
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(4)
Overly broad application takes a text as referring to topics the passage is not
talking about. <i>Thou shalt not kill</i> is cited as prohibiting capital
punishment, just war, self-defense, and eating meat. The commandment refers to
murder. [<u>wrong topics</u>]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(5)
Errors can come from stressing the wrong word, which sets up the wrong
contrast. <i>No one can come to me except the Father . . . <u>draws</u> him</i>
(John </span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'>6</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:44). As per context, the stress
should fall on <u>Father</u> rather than on <u>draw</u>: <i>No one can come to
me except the <u>Father</u> . . . draws him.</i> [<u>wrong contrast</u>]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(6)
Interpreters equivocate unlike things and then use a passage to address the
unlike thing. The Old Testament prohibition against eating blood has been used
to prohibit blood transfusions. [<u>equivocations</u>]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(7)
Readers fail to remember who the text is addressing. The problem shows up in
applying Mosaic commandments to Christiansas with tithing and Sabbath
directives, and in maintaining certain New Testament commands evidently meant within
an implied first-century cultural limitation of the Mediterranean worldrequiring
womens longer hair length in African Black races where hair length may naturally
be same for women and men.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>For
a more complete treatment of reasons for misinterpretation, see Pitfalls in
Interpretation in <i>HermeneuticsGetting the Point</i>, pp. 118-53. Regarding
some subtle issues, look at Selected Linguistic Phenomena, pp. 58-80, as well
as the rest of that work. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Genesis </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:26-27
[God is a combination of male and female.]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
text speaks of Gods creating mankind in his own image and then goes on to say
that he made <u>mankind</u> male and female. The verses no more apply to God
the male-female variant than they apply physicality in general to God. God is
spirit (John</span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> 4</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:24); spirit does not
have flesh and bones (Luke </span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>24</span></b><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:39). Scripture
gives no basis for the idea that sexuality somehow exists in the spirit realm.
There is no marriage there; people in the resurrection are like angels in those
respects (Matthew </span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>22</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:29-30; Hebrews </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:14).
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>See
also comments in <i>Old Testament Translation Notes </i>for <i>The
Conversational Old </i>Testament, pp. 4-5 regarding inclusive language.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Genesis
</span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>2</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:1-3
[Sabbath observance]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Gods
blessing the Sabbath and hallowing it is taken to mean that from the time of
creation, Sabbath observance was to be a permanent feature of human living, not
just an element of Jewish national and religious life. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Writing
from the viewpoint of his own time, Moses inserts here in Genesis a note connecting
Gods creation rest with Jewish national expectancies in his own time. The <u>reason</u>
for the Sabbath was to serve as a memorial of the exodusas a rest from
Egyptian bondage (Deuteronomy</span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> 5</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:15).
The <u>manner of observing</u> it was analogous to Gods resting from his
creative work (Exodus </span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>20</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:11).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See also comments
on Exodus </span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'>31</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:12-17.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Genesis </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>2</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:5-6 [No
rain before the flood]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See also
Hebrews </span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'>11</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:7.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Genesis </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>2</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:14-15
[Snakes have residual structures that formerly were connections for legs.]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Genesis </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>2</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:17
[forbidden fruit]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> In
popular talk, people have the habit of calling the forbidden fruit an apple,
but the text does not specify what fruit God forbade. People have also made
sexual relations what the forbidden fruit representedthe tree of <i>the
knowledge of good and evil.</i> Again, no such idea appears in the text. If it
comes into the picture at all, it comes from interpreters who consider the
material world as evil so that anything distinctively associated with its
operations is proscribed, physical desires included.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> This second
idea clearly removes the forbidden fruit from the rest of the story. The point
of making mankind male and female was to fill the earth (Genesis </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:28).
The fact that there is no comment about sexual relations prior to the Fall is a
happenstance of record, not an indication of prohibition.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Genesis</span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
2</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:18
[help meet]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Older
translations say help meet, meaning a helper appropriate to Adam. Since the
older expression has become unfamiliar, people today fudge it into helpmate
as an elaboration of mate. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Genesis </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>9</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:25
[curse of the black race]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The claim is
that the black race is accursed and doomed to servanthood because of the sin of
their ancestor Ham. But Noahs curse was pronounced on Canaan, not Ham, the
forefather of the African nations. The curse was pronounced because he saw the
nakedness of his father Noah and told his brothers Shem (Asian forefather) and
Japheth (European forefather) about it. The two brothers covered him up without
looking at his nakedness. When Noah got sober, he found out what Ham had done
and pronounced a curse, but he pronounced it on Canaan, Hams son, not on Ham: <i>Accursed
be Canaan; he will the lowest of servants to his brothers.</i> A connection is
made with the later inhabitants of Canaan, whom Israel conquered when God gave the
land to the Israelites, who descended from Shem. [a case of etiology]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Genesis </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>2</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:26-29
[men have one less rib than women]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Genesis</span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
38</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:8-10
[contraceptives] [onanism] [masturbation] [self-gratification]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The text has
been used to prohibit contraceptives and masturbation and to advocate sex for
procreation only. The issues tied to the occasion recorded either
over-interpret what is recounted, or they apply the situation to matters not like
it. The element considered common to the three issues is sexual
self-gratification. God struck Onan down because he was using his
sister-in-law instead of raising up children by her for his brother. Withdrawal
and its equivalentcontraceptivesinside a normal marriage situation come from
a different motivation. A second usage of <i>onanism</i> means masturbation in
English dictionaries, a matter not at issue here or addressed in scripture.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Exodus </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>16</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:14
(manna), 22-30 (quail) </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> As
part of belief in an eternal, universal Sabbath, these directives about the
gathering of quail and manna are invoked to argue that Sabbath was (supposed to
be) observed before Israel became a nationin contrast to the view that Sabbath
was a feature of national observance by Israel, a prescribed holiday that one
can agree had physical, psychological, social, and even spiritual benefits.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> But
Israel was not viewed as becoming a nation at the time the nation received the Law
at Sinai. It became a nation earlier at the crossing of the Red Sea (1
Corinthians </span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>10</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:1-2), when God separated
it from Egypt and its slave status and made it a separate, parallel nation (cp.
Hosea </span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'>11</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:1). God instituted Sabbath as a sign
between him and Israel: <i>You must observe my sabbaths. This will be a sign
between me and you for generations to come.</i> The commandment to observe
Sabbath in Exodus </span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>16</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> did precede the giving
of the Ten Commandments in Exodus </span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>20</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>,
but it was after crossing the Red Sea.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> There
is no evidence that the Sabbath was observed by Gods people before, outside,
or after national Israel.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Exodus </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>20</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:13
[capital punishment]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The text
says, <i>Thou shalt not kill,</i> in the familiar expression. It means <i>Do
not murder,</i> rather than kill in every conceivable sense. Elsewhere the
same Mosaic legislation provided for capital punishment for several crimes. See
Capital Crimes in Mosaism.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Exodus </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>21</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:24
[revenge]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>An eye
for an eye</i> served as a guideline for judges in measuring out punishment to
evil doers (cp. Leviticus </span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>24</span></b><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:20; Deuteronomy </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>19</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:21).
It states the <u>judicial principle</u> that the punishment should be
commensurate with the crime. Taking the rule out of the judicial setting and making
it a <u>social principle</u> turns it into justification for personal revenge. Jews
of Jesus day were evidently misapplying the principle in this way (Matthew </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>5</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:38-42).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Exodus</span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
31</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:12-17
[Sabbath observance]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i></i>[The
Sabbath] <i>is a sign between</i> [God] <i>and the children of Israel forever.</i>
Some Christians believe they should observe Sabbath because the word <i>forever</i>
appears in this quotation about hallowing it, particularly since they think of
the Sabbath as a memorial of <u>creation</u>. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(a)</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
Sabbath, however, was a memorial of the <u>exodus</u> from Egypt (meaning; Deuteronomy
</span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>5</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:15)
observed after the analogy of the creation rest (form): weekly, as a rest, on
the seventh day. That is why Exodus </span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>31</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:17
calls it a sign between him and children of Israel; Christians are not
Israelites or members of that nation. Moses wrote the explanatory comments in
Genesis </span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'>2</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:1-3 from his own viewpoint in timeafter
Israel was constituted a nation at the Red Sea (1 Corinthians </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>10</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:1-2).
Those comments do not mean that people from the time of creation prior to
Israel observed the seventh day of the week as the Sabbath or that Christians
after and outside of Israel should perpetuate the practice as a religious
principle.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(b)</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Forever</i>
means permanent within the frame of reference involved. It is an absolute word
used in the Old Testament, for example, also about a slave serving forever
(Exodus </span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'>21</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:6; cp. Philemon 15), about Solomons
temple being forever (1 Kings <b>8</b>:13; <b>9</b>:3, 5; 2 Chronicles <b>7</b>:16;
<b>30</b>:8), and other temporary things. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(c)</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> In
Colossians</span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'> 2</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:16-17 Paul tells his readers not to
let anyone judge them about meat, drink, feast days, new moons, or Sabbath days;
those were shadows of things to come. When the real came, the shadows were
removed (1 Corinthians</span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> 13</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:10).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>See
comments above on Genesis</span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> 2</span></b><span style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:1-3. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>See
<i>What the Bible Says About Salvation</i>, pp. 125-26.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Leviticus</span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
10</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:1-2</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
LORD struck Nadab and Abihu dead because they <i>offered strange fire on front
of the LORD, which he had not commanded them.</i> The instance is
universalized to mean that silence in regard to things associated with worship
prohibits them. This case with the strange fire, however, involves using
something <u>instead of</u> what God had commanded.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
formula for incense offered in the tabernacle was specifically indicated,
saying that no other formulation could be used. It also indicated that the
exact tabernacle formula could not be used for anywhere else.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Psalm </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>10</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:4
[atheists are fools] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>See
comments under Psalm </span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>14</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:1.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Psalm </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>14</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:1
(= </span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>53</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:1;
cp. </span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>10</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:4)
[Atheists are fools]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>A
food says in his heart, Theres no God. </i>The observation has been taken
to mean that a person without special revelation should be able to come to know
through nature around and within that God exists and to discover certain
important truths about him. The circumstance has called for using theistic
proofs for Gods existence (general revelation) and then using special
revelation to make him known more fully.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
foolishness, however, does not necessarily lie in not recognizing through
general revelation the existence and nature of the true God. The foolishness
lies in living sinfully and then trying to claim that there is no God to rein in
the behavior. The ulterior motive for denying Gods existence is
self-justification for exercising complete autonomy, and for hardening the
heart toward any reason the believer might offer as a corrective. The role of
theistic proofs may show a greater likelihood of divine existence than
alternative positions can muster, but it is another thing to claim that they
are adequate for positive proof for the typical person and therefore for
condemnation because of unbelief.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
passage does not say the person is a fool <u>because</u> he says there is not
God. It is just as likely that he is a fool because he wants to live how he
pleases and tries to ward off correction by saying there is no God there to
call him to task.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>See
also comments below on Romans</span><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> 1</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:16-23ff.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Psalm </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>53</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:1
[atheists are fools.] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Psalm </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>51</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:5
[original sin] [original guilt]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>David
says, <i>In sin my mother conceived me.</i> His statement has been taken to
mean that the guilt for Adams sin passed down to all his descendants, or the
somewhat lesser claim that Adams descendants all inherited a fallen nature because
of Adams sin and necessarily sin because of that fallen nature (natural
depravity). The inference is that peopleDavid and the rest of mankindare born
guilty of Adams sin or born with a fallen nature that necessarily leads to personally
sinning. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
text of Genesis does not say Adams descendants incurred the guilt of his sin,
nor does it indicate that his spiritual ability fell in a genetically
inheritable sense when he sinned. Sin is an act; an act cannot be transferred;
so its guilt and the penalty for it cannot be transferred to other people. What
is true by the nature of the case, Ezekiel <b>18</b>:4, 13b, 17, 20 also states
directly; guilt and penalty are personal: <i>The soul that sins is the one
that dies.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>As
far as Psalm <b>51</b> itself is concerned, David surely exaggerates for
emphasis on how thoroughly sinful he felt over the Bathsheba incident. He used similar
wording in Psalm<b> 58</b>:3 about babies coming forth from the womb telling
lies. Babies no more tell lies from birth than David was conceived in sin by
his married parents or made to trust God when he was on his mothers breasts
(Psalm<b> 71</b>:5-6). If born in sins means natural depravity, then <i>trusting
God on his mothers breasts</i> means natural goodness. Interpreters cannot
have it both ways. Born in means thoroughly characterized by, as when people
say, <i>Hes a born liar, Hes a born salesman.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>A
similar misuse of this text occurred on the lips of religious leaders in Jesus
day when they claimed that misfortune comes from sin (poetic justice). When a
man born blind responded cogently to accusations about Jesus healing him from
blindness, they told him, <i>You were altogether born in sin, and you are
trying to teach us?</i> (John <b>9</b>:34; cp. 1-2). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>See
also Job <b>14</b>:4; <b>15</b>:14; Psalm <b>71</b>:5-6; Ephesians <b>2</b>:3. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>See
also Deuteronomy <b>24</b>:16; Isaiah <b>3</b>:11; Jeremiah <b>31</b>:30.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Psalm<b> 53</b>:1 [Atheists
are fools.] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>See
comments on Psalm <b>14</b>:1.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Psalm <b>93</b>:1 [The
earth is flat.]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See also
Isaiah <b>11</b>:12; Revelation <b>7</b>:1</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Psalms <b>103</b>:3 [divine
healing]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>[Yahveh]
heals all your diseases.</i> That God heals all diseases of people that have
full faith (a) goes beyond the necessary and intended meaning of Davids
words, (b) does not fit the rest of biblical teaching about sickness and
healing, and (c) does not match Christian experience. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(a)
The Psalm requires proper word stress (the law of emphasis). It extols the
character of <u>God</u> and credits <u>him</u> with the good things of life. In
so doing, the passage calls for stressing the right element: not <u>all</u> (your
diseases), but <u>God</u>. It is <u>God</u> who heals all your sicknesses [that
are healed], forgives all your sins [that are forgiven], gives benefits [that are
received], redeems your life from destruction [when redeemed], satisfies your
desires [when satisfied], renews your youth [when renewed], executes judgments
for the oppressed [when so executed] (2-6); so <i>Bless <u>Yahveh</u></i> (<b>103</b>:1).
He is a forgiving God who does not constantly chide or stay angry permanently. He
is slow to get anger, merciful, gracious, and abundant in lovingkindness
(8-11): he understands our frame (14). It is not that he cures <u>all</u>
your diseases, so all faithful people never ail. Rather, <u>God</u> is the
(ultimate) one who cures all the diseases you have been healed from. The real
source of all remedies lies beyond your own efforts and beyond people who
assist you. So to speak, take care of yourself, thank the doctors, but bless <u>God</u>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(b)
<i>All your diseases</i> is absolute in <u>form</u>, but not necessarily in
meaning. The comment does not state the full picture any more than <i>forgiving
all your sins</i> does. There are additional qualifying factors natural to
such issuesfactors scripture explains elsewhere. God intentionally built
sickness and misfortuneculminating in deathinto the human circumstance to make
it obvious unequivocally that we are not fully in charge of our existence and
that we are not self-sufficient. We are to learn that <i>power is made perfect
in weakness </i>(2 Corinthians <b>12</b>:9).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(c)
Relevant <u>examples</u> or unhealed maladies in the faithful are Trophimus,
whom Paul left at Miletus sick (2 Timothy <b>4</b>:20), and Pauls own <i>thorn
in the flesh</i> that God did not remove when Paul asked him three times to
take it away (2 Corinthians <b>12</b>:7-9). Jesus prayed three times in Gethsemane
for the Father to take away they cup he was about to drink, but it did not
happen</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See
also Exodus <b>15</b>:26; Psalm <b>30</b>:2; Jeremiah <b>30</b>:17.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>See
also the study of all as used in scripture: <i>Interpretation: Getting the
Point</i>: all. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Psalm <b>111</b>:9 [Preachers
should not be called reverend.] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
term <i>reverend</i> occurs only once in the King James translation, and it is
in reference to God. So, some think it should be reserved for him. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
same word (in the same Hebrew Niphal stem) refers to people in Isaiah <b>18</b>:2,
7, who are <i>a terrible people.</i> Psalm <b>139</b>:14 says we are <i>fearfully
and terribly made.</i> The root verb simply means <i>to fear.</i> There is
something to be said for preachers and other religious workers not to be caught
up in expecting to be addressed with honorifics, but the word <i>reverend</i>
is no more objectionable than any other respectful term of address.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Proverbs <b>13</b>:34 [corporal
punishment]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> In
reaction against child abuse, it has become fashionable to forbid corporal
punishment altogether. The two things are not the same, of course, but they are
treated as if they were. The text here is re-handled as if the rod is not for
corporal punishment, but for guidance. The idea comes from mixing two unrelated
issues: the discipline of children and the managing of sheep. The rod is
supposedly used to guide little lambs along the path the shepherd wants them to
follow. But, that is not what a shepherds rod was for; it was for fending off
other animals that might attack the sheep, which are relatively helpless. The
shepherds staff (with its crook) is the more likely tool for moving sheep
around or guiding them. The only connection between the two circumstances is
the word rod. The situation is a case of wanting to bend biblical teaching
into something acceptable to a cultural idea that in itself is not appropriate.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Several
passages in scripture speak to the corporal-punishment issue. Those passages
cannot be removed from biblical teaching by the equivocation advanced above:
Hebrews <b>12</b>:5-11 speaks extensively about the parallel between a fathers
corporal punishment of a child and a way of looking at the experiences of
persecution. See also Proverbs<b> 19</b>:18; <b>22</b>:15; <b>23</b>:13-14; <b>29</b>:15,
17. <i>Using a rod on him will not kill him,</i> and other phraseology in
these texts do not picture gentile guidance given to a rebellious son.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Proverbs<b> 23</b>:31 [total
abstinence]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Look
not on the wine when it is red.</i> The admonition serves to undergird the commonly
held belief that drinking alcohol is forbidden period (excepting medicinal
purposes, perhaps)total abstinence.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Instead,
the greater likelihood is that the passage advises rather than commands. In
other words, it is a good idea to just leave wine alone. It is another thing to
forbid it altogether. Staring at a cup of wine with desire or resignation
presents a dangerous form of attachment to it.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> In Romans <b>14</b>:21
Paul says that he would not drink wine or eat meat if it caused a brother to
stumble. The implication surely is that he would no more abstain from wine
under other circumstances than he would abstain from meat (cp. Luke <b>7</b>:33-34).
If a Christian need not be a vegetarian, he need not be a tea-totaler. The
advice still stands that alcohol brings with it the dangers of addiction and
lack of ability to carry on personal relationships appropriately. The advice
holds true especially in the modern situation where the proof of alcohol
beverages can be much higher than the natural fermentation available in the
ancient world.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Isaiah<b> 11</b>:12 [The
earth is flat.] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See also
Psalm <b>93</b>:1; Revelation <b>7</b>:1</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Malachi <b>3</b>:10 [Christian
tithing] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Bring
all the tithes into the storehouse.</i> The reference is commonly used as a
basis for obligating Christians to tithe their earnings.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
commandment, however, comes from the Mosaic economy about the tithe as the basic
element of the taxation system for the Jewish nation, which was a union of
church and state. Malachi is not speaking to Christians. Applying his words to
the church would be artificial because the church has no national component;
civil matters are delivered to secular jurisdiction and financed by local,
state, and national taxes. Christians are not under the Mosaic Law (Hebrews<b>
8</b>:6-13 &lt; Jeremiah <b>31</b>:31-34;<i> etc</i>.). Tithing is never
commanded in the church age; transferring the Mosaic tithe to the Christian
church involves confusing covenants as well as making an apples-and-oranges equivocation.
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>In
the Christian economy, giving is no more quantified than any other spiritual
act like frequency of praying, fasting, fellowshipping, Bible reading,
witnessing; or the number of times to forgive (Matthew <b>18</b>:21). Such
matters are measured relative to real need, a donors ability, and
opportunity (Mark <b>14</b>:7), not by abstract rule. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
Christian dispensation raises the ante by moving from quantification to reality
factors: ability, commitment, and need. It is like <i>doing greater things
than these</i> (miracles)<i> </i>(John <b>14</b>:12), which included
resurrections. A person could hardly do something greater than raising someone
from the dead. The greater works Jesus disciples would do are made possible by
moving to a greater kind of category, a spiritual onemoving to a higher kind
of category rather than a greater degree within the same category. Saving a
soul from death is greater than healing his leprosy, so to speak. In that same
way, Christian gifting is greater because it is to be done in a greater kind of
operation: reality rather than rules, from a higher motivation rather than more
quantity.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew <b>2</b>:11 [the
Magi/Wise men]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The Magi came
to the house where Jesus was, not to the stable. Perhaps for efficiency,
Christmas cards conflate the visits of the shepherds and the wise men as both
occurring at the stable where the Lord was born. But the coming of these men
from the East was a little while at least after his birth. If the star appeared
to them at the time Jesus was born, it may have been a month or two before they
reached Bethlehem. Ezra <b>7</b>:9 records that Ezra left Babylon on April 8
and arrived in Jerusalem on August 4, a period of about 87 days. If the Magi
were from that general area in the East, we have some idea of the order of
magnitude for their travel time.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew<b> 2</b>:1-12 [number
of wise men]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Matthew does
not specify how many wise men came to Bethlehem, nor does he give their names. The
number three grew up in tradition probably from the fact that they brought
three gifts that scripture mentions: gold, frankincense, and myrrh. Tradition
further elaborated the account by developing names for the Magi: Melchior,
Gaspar, and Balthasar.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew<b> 5</b>:17-18 [distinction
of covenants]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>I
did not come to destroy the Law, but to fulfill it</i> does not mean that
Christians are still under the Law of Moses. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Jesus
does not say, <i>I did not come to destroy, but to perpetuate it.</i> What he
is getting ready to teach in this sermon does not imply that the Law should be
cast aside (destroy) as wrong; he came to accomplish the intent of the Law in
embodying righteousness himselfa higher kind of righteousness especially than
taught and practiced by the scribes and Pharisees in their legalizing,
quantifying approach to behavior before God. Once he accomplished the real basis
for righteousness, the legal codification of divine will would be removed, not
as false, but as no longer having jurisdiction over the people of God (Ephesians
<b>2</b>:15). Christ now embodies righteousness in his person, and persons
identify with him as the basis for their being viewed as like him, that is, as
righteous. Christianity comes in the train of Mosaism, but it is not itself a
legal system; the moral principles included in the Law have been
re-instituted in the New Covenant, which provides them with an interpersonal
context and nature. This New Covenant is not only <u>another covenant</u>, but <u>another
kind of covenant</u> (Jeremiah <b>31</b>:31-35; cp. Hebrews <b>8-10</b>).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> A
similar sentiment in Luke <b>16</b>:17 has as its preceding verse, <i>The law
and the prophets were until John </i>[the Baptist]. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Not
destroy</i> must mean <i>not get rid of</i> in the sense that it is not
divine or true or good. Jesus did not want his listeners to get that impression
from what he was about to tell them: <i>you have heard, but I say. </i>The
contrast with <i>destroying it</i> is fulfilling it; hence, the law is not
false. In fact, it prepared for the gospel (Galatians <b>3</b>:24) by defining
and sensitizing to sin (Romans<b> 5</b>:20; Galatians <b>3</b>:19).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
New American Standard Bible reads <i>I did not come to annul </i>the law. Such
English wording would normally imply that we are still under Mosaism because it
has not been annulled. The wording could perhaps stand if a reader were to
take Jesus full statement as a relative negative, <i>I did not come so much
to destroy the law as to fulfill it.</i> Such a sentiment would probably not
occur to an English readers mind, however. Besides, destroy would still
carry the implication that the Law was false. Sometimes the law-gospel relationship
interpreters picture the Law as amended by the gospel. That way, amendments
remove Mosaic elements that do not continuecivil and ceremonial mattersand new
elements are amended in. That takes care of the issue in part, but it does not
take care of the change in kind between the covenants from legal to
interpersonal. Not only are Christians under a <u>different</u> covenant; they
are under a different <u>kind of covenant</u>, one written on the heart, as
Jeremiah prophesied it. Besides, not everything in the Law has been removed
item by item that Christians no longer observe. The law of jealousy, for
example, has not been specifically removed (Numbers <b>5</b>:11-31).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See
also The Distinction Between Covenants and Some Relevant Applications: A
Sketch.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew <b>5</b>:32
[divorce; remarriage of the innocent party]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Whoever
marries her that is put away commits adultery </i>is taken to mean that
someone divorced by the spouseso the spouse can marry someone elsecannot get
married again; anyone marrying such a wrongfully divorced person would be
committing adultery.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Such
an understanding jumps over the immediately preceding material and connects it instead
with the more distant general divorce comment at the front of <b>19</b>:32. In
contrast to the idea that Mosaism allowed for a husbands divorcing his spouse
for any reason whatever, Jesus speaks in terms of adultery as grounds for
divorce. In that context, (a) divorcing her implies that she has committed
adultery. <i>Makes her an adulteress </i>compares to <i>makes God a liar</i>
(1 John <b>1</b>:10); that is, he treats her like an adulteress. It is
difficult to imagine a sensible, practical meaning that would make an innocent
woman guilty of something she did not do. Some have supposed that Jesus meant the
possibility thatfrom a practical standpointshe would be forced to remarry to
have a means of support. Being forced to do what Jesus was forbidding to do
would establish a self-contradictory situation. One bailout might be to suppose
a contrast between basic theory (remarriage is adultery) and practical
necessity (remarriage for support).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Jesus
subsequent comment about remarriage comes in the train of the adultery
situation he has established as his context: (b) Marrying an adulteress implies
that her adultery does not matter to the second husband; that makes him as bad
as she is: <i>he commits adultery.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Strictly
speaking, the passage does not deal with (1) remarriage of an innocent party,
(2) any additional appropriate exceptions, or (3) the impact salvation has on a
guilty partys options. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
Lords brief comments about divorce <i>for adultery</i> do not necessarily
mean (3) <i>only for adultery.</i> Paul comments in 1 Corinthians <b>7</b>:15
that a spouse is not <i>under bondage</i> if an unbelieving spouse <i>leaves</i>
the believer. <i>Under bondage</i> evidently means bondage to the marriage
contract (rather than <i>under bondage</i> of guilt perhaps). Desertion,
then, as a second ground for divorce establishes a class of causes appropriate
to the marital institution: abuse, homosexual involvement, polygamy, desertion
in the sense of refusing sexual relations (cp. 1 Corinthians <b>7</b>:1-7), and
perhaps other behaviors that deny the distinctive nature of marriage itself. These
additional possibilities become less black and white because other
considerations contribute to what conclusions are appropriate.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew <b>5</b>:33-37 [oath
taking] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
words, extracted from their setting, could forbid taking oaths in legal
settings like in court.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Do
not swear at all, not by . . . .</i> Jesus addresses the habit of swearing
oaths about everything in typical social situations instead of just speaking
truthfully, meaning it, and acting accordingly. <i>Your word should be your
bond.</i> Yes or No is sufficient. At his trial before Caiaphas, Jesus answered
when the high priest said, <i>I adjure you by the living God; tell us whether
you are the Christ, the Son of God. </i>So to speak, by responding to the
adjuration, Jesus let the high priest put him under oath.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
whole Sermon on the Mount (Matthew <b>5-7</b>) treats everyday relationships with
people and God in the heavenly (read spiritual, interpersonal) kingdom Christ
came to establish. He was concerned at the time with social conduct, not judicial
process (<b>5</b>:33-37), physical abuse, or armed conflict (<b>5</b>:38-42).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>See
also James <b>5</b>:12. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew<b> 5</b>:33-3
[cursing]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Do not
swear at all, not by . . . .</i> Cursing is another kind of problem that Jesus
does not address here. The English word <i>swear</i> covers oath-taking <u>and</u>
cursing, but the former is the Lords topic at this point. Filthy talk is
prohibited elsewhere: Ephesians <b>4</b>:29; <b>5</b>:4; Colossians<b> 3</b>:8;
2 Peter <b>2</b>:7. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew <b>5</b>:39
[pacifism] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> As
with the whole Sermon on the Mount, <i>Turn the other cheek</i> is directed
at everyday social relations. Self-defense against physical threat and
endangerment is another, complex matter. Jesus uses a graphic way of resisting
the propensity to <i>fight at the drop of a hat</i> or to take revenge for
every perceived slight or put-down. Dont respond in kind. Later Jesus tells
his disciples to sell their cloaks and buy swords, not as means of advancing
the kingdom of God, but for self-protection (Luke <b>22</b>:36).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> John
the Baptist did not tell the soldiers who asked him what they should do, <i>Get
out of the army.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See Matthew <b>28</b>:18-20;
Mark<b> 16</b>:15-16; Acts <b>1</b>:8.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew <b>7</b>:1 [judging]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Judge
not, that you be not judged</i> is taken to forbid saying that what someone
else is doing is wrong; that is Gods business. Each person is supposed to take
care of himself. Saying someone is sinning amounts to hate speech.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Judge
not, lest you be judged,</i> however, prohibits criticizing, finding fault,
looking for weaknesses and making a big point of them. Such behaviors exhibit a
negative attitude toward the person, an attitude born of a competitive spirit that
seeks self-worth by superiority. When people do that, other people hold them to
the same standards they press against them. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>A
person has to judge in the sense of evaluating whether some act is sinful and
whether other people are sinning when they do it. He has to evaluate those whom
Jesus calls swine in the following verses; otherwise, he will not be choosing
his friends. From a practical standpoint, of course, it is better to focus on
the sin than on the sinner; doing so, removes the stigma of arrogance and avoids
an excuse for others not to change.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew <b>12</b>:36 [jesting]
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>In
the day of judgment people will give an account of every idle word theyve spoken.</i>
The wording has been understood as prohibiting levity, exclamations <i>for
Petes sake</i> as if they were necessarily replacements for swear words
[swearing by proxy]. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
comment, however, is not meant to prohibit humor, telling jokes, or having fun as
such. Why would God give people a capacity for humor and then not allow any
expression of it? The warning does not proscribe using meaningless words or
even senseless sounds, meaningless (non-cursing) expletives, exclamations (for
expressing feelings or emotions). Those other, propriety matters are governed by
circumstance and the connotations that have become associated with each
expression.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> In
this setting, the Pharisees had accused Jesus of casting out demons by the
power of Beelzebub. Idle words may refer to the Pharisees casting around for
any old explanation for his exorcisms. They may not have been all that confident
in their Beelzebub claim, but they wanted to say somethinganythingto
neutralize his influence as attached to such demonstrations. The more negative
spin they could put on it the better. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Jesus
warning could eliminate talk. Off-handed, thoughtless comments come under
evaluation as surely as any other kind of expression. People cannot say, <i>Oh,
I didnt mean anything by it</i>; <i>I misspoke.</i> Jesus noted that people
are judged by what they say, whether they supposedly mean it or not, believe it
or not, have their hearts in it or not, intend to do it or not (empty promises).
People cannot just mouth what other people want to hear, what creates
impressions, what neutralizes the situation, <i>what gets you off the hook. </i>Lying
is lying; blasphemy is blasphemy. Words cant be just talk to mislead,
confuse, sound wise, or create impressions. People are not to <i>talk through
their hat</i>; they must mean what they say. (cp. Luke <b>24</b>:11, </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>;
Matthew<b> 12</b>:36 uses </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
for idle.)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>See
also Ephesians<b> 5</b>:4.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew <b>13</b>:24-30,
36-43 [anti premillennialism]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The owner
tells the foreman to gather up the tares <u>first</u> and burn them. Some have
taken the wording that argues against premillennialism, </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>which
pictures Christs coming to deliver his saints from the wicked, who are left in
the world. It is not wise to take wording from other circumstances and apply
them to a persons own topic of interest. They may differ in ways the original
author was not concerned to avoid misunderstanding about. A person would expect
gathering one to precede gathering the other; the choice might simply be
arbitrary here. The prioritizing reason only comes into the interpretation
because of a construction of future history that the reader has developed from
whatever bases. If that construction is not correct, this observation does not
have any value in discussing the issue. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew <b>13</b>:47 [the
church as a mixed community of saved and lost]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Jesus
said that the kingdom of heaven is like a net thrown into the sea and gathers
every kind. The text has been used in defense of the mixed church membership created
by infant baptism and church-state unification; it is composed of every kind of
person.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Jesus
point is that the kingdom is <u>open to all</u> in the sense that the gospel is
cast out to all to be gathered in. That is different from being <u>comprised
of </u>all. Obviously, those in the sea (world) who do not conform to the
nature and expectations of the kingdom do not become part of the kingdom. If
that were not the case, there would be no difference between the church and the
world.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The parable
goes on to say that the good are gathered into the boat, <u>but the bad are
thrown away</u>. Involved in this situation also is the question of whether
local church membership is an advisable practice.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>16</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:18
[</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>the
primacy of Peter] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Jesus
says to Peter, <i>You are Peter, and I will build my church on that rock.</i>
The statement serves as a basis for the idea that Peter was the primary apostle
on whom the church was built. His successors (the popes) are, therefore, the
head of the church on earth. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
text does not say, <i>I will build my church on <u>you</u>, Peter,</i> but <i>You
are Peter, and I will build my church <u>on that </u>rock.</i> The content of
the confession is what stands in third person to both Jesus and Peter: that
rock. That truth is what Peter came through with when he uttered his great
confession, and that truth is what he was to proclaim. Jesus means, <i>As
surely as your name means rock, I will build my church on the firm fact that
I am the Messiah, the Son of the living God, and even death and the grave will
not stop me from building it</i>a message that Peter and his fellow apostles
were to preach.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew<b> 16</b>:19
[doctrinal origination regarding church structure and Christian practice]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> (a)
Keys are for getting into something, in this case, getting into the kingdom. The
binding and loosing on earth and in heaven is in regard to matters of the keys
of kingdom, <i>i.e</i>., salvation matters. Peter (and the other apostles) were
to use the keys Christ would give them, not originate their own.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(b)
What Peter and the other apostles bound and did on earth was done by
proclaiming the message God gave them, <i>i.e</i>., in regard to what
originated with God, not what would originate with them. (The rare future
perfect pass, used here, may reflect that priority of divine origination: will
have been bound-loosed. It more likely intends to convey the certainty of
divine follow-through in consequence of the apostles work.) The apostles bound
what <u>obligations</u> people were to observe; they loosed whatever people
were notor were no longer obligatedto observe (out of the Mosaic system?). The
apostles loosed peoples <u>sins</u> when they gave them an opportunity to
respond to the message about forgiveness; they bound peoples sins on them when
people refused the message that could take those sins away. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew <b>16</b>:19 [clerical
absolution]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
accompanying promise is that whatever Peter would bind or loose on earth God
would surely bind or loose in heaven. Christs promise is used to undergird the
idea that God has given his representativesin the persons of the clergythe
right and responsibility to forgive other peoples sins. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(a)
The comments, however, are capable of an appropriate alternative explanation
and so do not provide a positive basis for clerical absolution. The Lord
presumably refers to loosing or binding sin in response to the <u>proclamation</u>
of his own message (in principle), not a binding and loosing by Peters <u>pronouncement</u>
of forgiveness (on individual persons). The loosing or binding comes from peoples
acceptance or rejection of Peters <u>proclamation</u>. Speaking to all
apostles later, Jesus promises the same thing (Matthew <b>18</b>:18) when
referring to <u>prayer</u> and to <u>church discipline</u> for sinning. The <i>binding
or loosing in heaven </i>corresponds with the promise at the Great Commission
that he would be with them to the end of the age. At the <u>insufflation</u>
(John <b>20</b>:22), after another statement of commission, Jesus associates
the binding and loosing with receiving the Holy Spirit, which, among a complex
of other things, involved the willingness to preach forgiveness to the very
ones who killed their Lord and would persecute them. The requisite spirit
contrasts with James and Johns earlier mentality of wanting to call down fire on
the Samaritans (Luke <b>9</b>:54; cp. Mark <b>3</b>:17, Boanerges, sons of
thunder). <i>You do not know what spirit you are of, </i>Jesus told them (Luke<b>
9</b>:55b, textual variant).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>God
sticks to his promise to stand by his followers when they present the message
he has given them to present (bind) and the promises it contains (loose). God
carries through on his part of the process he devises. Stating that promise was
important because they might not expect persecution, suffering, and death since
they were fulfilling their responsibility. Such rejection by other people will
not indicate abandonment by God or that nothing divine is happening as a result
of their work. Their work would have an intangible character; so success was
not necessarily measurableas would have been the case were Christ to be
instituting a national kingdom. The binding amounts to an assurance that the
sins of those who reject the apostles message and persecute them for
proclaiming it will not get away with anything. Such sins will not be
forgiven in this world/age or the next.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(b)
Clerical absolution is contrary to the nature of personal sin. Sin is against
heaven and the other immediate person (in your sight; Luke <b>15</b>:18,
21). It is against God in that he establishes the moral guidelines, and it is against
anyone harmed by someones failure to observe the values applicable to the
situation. The Christian system is not under lawa legal standard abstracted
from the one who gave it; so there is no corresponding legal authority attached
to the abstracted standard by which a third party could involve itself by
removing legal guilt for a breach of a legal standard. In the Christian
understanding, sin is between persons; it is interpersonal; hence, there is no
natural way to involve third parties. Only if sin had a legal component could
there be a place to absolve by authority a breach of standard. Even the Jews
understood that no one could really forgive sin except God (Mark <b>2</b>:7). Real
forgiveness cannot be pronounced by anyone other than the person sinned against.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>See
<i>What the Bible Says About Salvation</i>, pp. 401-2).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew<b> 18</b>:18 [clerical
absolution]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See comments
under Matthew <b>16</b>:19 and John <b>20</b>:21.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew <b>19</b>:8 [=
Mark <b>10</b>:6] [fiat creationism]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
Pharisees asked Jesus whether it was lawful to divorce a wife for every reason.
Jesus answered that from the beginning God made male and female (<b>19</b>:4-5)
with the comment, <i>What God has joined together man must not separate. </i>The
follow-up question was why Moses allowed divorce. Jesus responded that Moses
allowed it <i>because of the hardness of mens hearts</i> [civil regulation], <i>but
from the beginning it has not been so</i> [moral expectancy]<i>. </i></span></p>
