<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>From
the beginning has been taken to argue that the creation of mankind could not
have been but a very short time since the beginning of the whole creation or
Jesus could not have worded his comment the way he did. The statement in
Matthew <b>19</b>:8 is taken in reference to the wrong beginning. Jesus need not
be taken as a reference to the beginning of all Gods creative activity; he is
taking about the beginning of mankindthe beginning of the marital situation in
contrast to the time of Moses, when he wrote the divorce provision into Jewish
law. From the beginning indicates that from the <u>beginning of creating
mankind</u>, God made them male and female. The inference favoring fiat
creation applies the Lords words more broadly than what he is talking about. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew<b> 19</b>:12
[castration for the kingdoms sake]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
Lord comments that some men are born eunuchs, some are made eunuchs by other men,
and some make themselves eunuchs for the kingdom of heavens sake. The wording
for the third set could be taken literally to mean self-castration for Christs
sakerather than as remaining sexually inactive in the single state. If Jesus
had meant remaining sexually inactive, there were easier, cleared ways to say
it, supposedly. (See <i>e.g</i>., <i>BAR</i>, spring, 2015, pp.)</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
fact that Jesus could have expressed sexual inactivity some other way does not
mean, of course, he could not express it <u>this</u> wayin analogical terms. Besides,
Jesus is carrying forward his teaching about marriage and divorce in response
to Peters comment about not getting married if a person cannot get out of a
bad situation afterwards. The alternatives in that connection have to do with
being able to remain unmarried, an option Jesus sayspresumably from a
practical standpointnot every man can fulfill. Castration, however, is
something every man could have done; it was an extreme measure forced on men
under special circumstances (as for ministering to a harem or in a household
with exposed female persons). There is nothing about the nature or purpose of
Christs kingdom that calls for such a measure. The notion that the physical is
somehow evil does not belong in Christian thought.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Furthermore,
Pauls solution to the issue raised by biological drives was not castration,
but marriage. The alternatives were to marry or burn with desire 1
Corinthians <b>7</b>:9). He makes these comments in the same general
circumstance of serving the church without distraction in the face of eminent
persecution.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew <b>20</b>:1-16
[equal rewards]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Everyone
gets the same reward just as all the workers in the vineyard at the end of the
day got the same pay regardless of how long they worked. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>On
the contrary, the parable means that salvation is by grace, using equal pay for
all rather than quantified hourly wages as the vehicle of expression.
Quantification does not fit easily into interpersonal matters because attitude
and motive are not objectively quantifiable.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>There
appear to be levels of greatness in the kingdom (Matthew <b>20</b>:26-28),
which could correspond then to levels of reward in the eschaton. That reward,
however, may be greater responsibility, not greater privilege (ten cities,<i>
etc</i>.). There is the left and right hand of the throne, but those places are
not mine to give, Jesus says. (Matthew <b>20</b>:23).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>In
general, however, a person has to wonder, not only how you can quantify
greatness in this life, but how you quantify it in the afterlife.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew
<b>20</b>:28</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
Lord gave his life as a ransom for many. The statement is taken to indicate the
manner in which Christs atonement operates. His soul/life is payment to redeem
us from Satans power.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> However,
ransoms are paid to someone for the freedom of a slave or to get back someone
kidnapped. In the case of salvation, the lostness is alienation from God, not
possession or ownership by Satan, legitimate or otherwise. Christs death is
here pictured as having the same <u>effect</u> as paying a ransom does: it frees
from an unwanted condition where the person in need does not contribute to the
resulting freedom. It is not the kind of difficulty that can be paid by another,
because sin and its consequences are personal and interpersonal, not having to
do with a legal status. Ransom, purchase, and other images for the value of
Christs atonement have in common the element that the recipient does not
produce the result, in this case, relationship to God, reconciliation, being
saved by another.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew<b> 21</b>:22
[answered prayer]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>All
things whatever you ask in prayer, believing, you will receive.</i> The
generalized promise is taken to mean that all requests to God in Christs name
will be granted; and if they are not, some explanation is required; especially
the no is because of a deficiency of faith (cp. Matthew <b>17</b>:19-21). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(a)
<u>Experience</u> does not seem to bear out such an interpretation, and people
can always suppose their faith or earnestness was inadequate since faith and sincerity
are degree matters not readily measurable and always subject to improvement. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(b)
The rest of <u>scripture</u> evidently shows that no is possible since the
Father declined Jesus threefold prayer in Gethsemane. God likewise denied
Pauls threefold prayer for removing his thorn in the flesh; surely faith was
not deficient in these cases, Gods <i>grace was sufficient for him</i> as he
was. Having the thorn helped avoid pride (2 Corinthians <b>12</b>:9). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>(c)
The gap between perceived promise and observed experience may be somewhat
bridged over by saying that other answers exist: if it accords with the rest of
Gods will, not now, if you do your part, if you are forgiving others (Matthew <b>6</b>:15),
if your motives are right (James <b>4</b>:3), if you are treating other people
right (1 Peter <b>3</b>:7), if it is in earnest (Luke <b>18</b>:8), if you have
proper respect for God (Matthew<b> 8</b>:18); he may give something better in
substitution or the functional equivalent in the afterlife. No might apply
also to cases where the request would reduce the potential for growth. The one
praying may have a good enough situation (Gods grace is sufficient for him; 2
Corinthians<b> 12</b>:9); granting the request would unnecessarily reduce
growth potential (2 Corinthians <b>12</b>:7, 9; cp. Job <b>1-2</b>; Acts <b>4</b>:29,
31; 2 Corinthians <b>10</b>:13b; <b>12</b>:8-9). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Another
observation bridging the promise-experience gap is that such passages may mean all
kinds of things rather than all things in number. In Matthew <b>7</b> the
context speaks of Gods not giving a supplicant something contrary to what he
wants or needs. Matthew <b>17</b> and Mark <b>11</b> talk about moving
mountains by prayer, as if the point is that no matter how extreme the request
might be, it is available through prayer.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See Matthew <b>7</b>:7-8
(= Luke <b>11</b>:9-10); <b>17</b>:19-20; <b>18</b>:19; Mark <b>11</b>:24; John
<b>14</b>:13; (<b>15</b>:7); <b>15</b>:16b; <b>16</b>:23-24; James <b>1</b>:6-7;
1 John <b>3</b>:22;<b> 5</b>:14.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew <b>23</b>:9 [honorifics]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Do
not call (anyone) father on the earth because one is your father, the heavenly
one.</i> Jesus speaks similarly about masters and rabbis; he does so after
condemning those who try to create an aura of superiority. So, the point is not
so much about the terms themselves, but about using them to exalt oneself. The
manner of speaking here may represent relative negative, which creates relative
emphasis by total denial. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>People
do well not to make themselves subservient to other people whether by address
or behavior that implies obsequiousness. In this text, religious leaders are
particularly warned not to vaunt themselves up by special dress, expected salutation,
or obeisance from others, who actually are their equals in <u>value</u>. But
self-abasement is a different thing from respecting people in authority (Romans
<b>13</b>:7). Anyone who does not have such <u>authority</u> should not expect
special treatment, and those who have it should not expect it outside their
sphere of operation.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> A
person does question the propriety of calling someone father who was not in
fact responsible for his conversionas Paul was to Timothy (1 Timothy <b>1</b>:2).
The propensity toward titular proliferation in the religious arena does not
bode well with the spirit of these warnings.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Jesus
populates his parables with people who say father: father Abraham (Luke <b>24</b>:24),
the prodigal son that says<i><u>,</u> <u>Father, I have sinned against heaven
and in your sight</u></i> (Luke<b> 15</b>:18).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew <b>23</b>:23 [tithing]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Jesus
criticized the religious leaders for meticulous tithing while leaving more
important matters undone. <i>These you ought to have done and not to have left
the other undone</i> (= Luke <b>11</b>:42); they should have been doing both. So
Christian should tithe, it is said. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Jesus
conducted his ministry while the Mosaic economy was still operating; he was not
overthrowing Old Covenant requirements as such, regardless of the perversions
these men had brought in (<b>23</b>:1-39). At the start of this denunciation,
he told the audiences to observe whatever the scribes and Pharisees told them <i>because
they sit in Moses seat</i> (<b>23</b>:2). That blanket admonition would obligate
Christians to all the Mosaic requirements and the traditions of the elders if
indeed this passage served as a basis for Christian observancesuch as tithing.
Jesus addresses, not Christians, but his Jewish contemporaries by the command. In
the new dispensation, however, no portion belongs to God in any sense that is
not also true of the whole. There is no golden portion. Similarly, we do not
have altars that we bring our gifts to (<b>23</b>:18; cp. Matthew <b>5</b>:23-24)
or a temple whose gold we can swear by (<b>23</b>:16-17).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>These
comments about giving apply to spiritual activities in general. We do not ask
how many times to forgive a repentant brother (Matthew <b>18</b>:21-22), how
many times a day to pray (Acts <b>3</b>:1; <b>10</b>:9, 30), how many times a
week to fast (Luke <b>19</b>:9-14), and the like. If some obligation is not
addressed to Christians, it is not part of our manner of operation. Without
special accompanying information, if matters from Jesus ministry do not appear
in the teaching from Pentecost and afterward, they are not of Christian
obligation or guidance.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Cp.
Luke <b>11</b>:42.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew
<b>23</b>:37 = Luke<b> 13</b>:34 [the feminine in deity]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Jesus
speaks of his efforts to bring the people of Jerusalem together to him. He
compares those efforts to a chicken trying to gather her chicks under her
wings. From this comparison some readers have wanted to claim that Jesus has
the element of the element of the feminine in him.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Such
an inference is called allegorizing the comparison, carrying an authors
comparison beyond the point of the author notes. When that happens, the reader
loses the authors authority for whatever claims the reader is making.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> It
would be as legitimate to infer in this case that God is bipedal with feathers or
that in the flesh Jesus was a bisexual. It would be like arguing there can be
female apostles because Paul told the Galatians he was in travail till Christ
was formed in them (Galatians <b>4</b>:19). If readers want to make such a
point, they do so on their own authority; it does not derive from this text.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Besides,
the male-female variant does not apply to the God of the Bible. In one of his
resurrection appearance, Jesus tells his disciples that they are not looking at
a spirit because <i>spirit does not have flesh and blood like you see I have</i>
(Luke <b>24</b>:39); in John <b>4</b>:24 Jesus tells the woman at the well that
<i>God is spirit/a-spirit.</i> Sex is a characteristic of the physical order,
not the spiritual.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Other
instances of trying to attach female sexuality to God include comparisons to a servants
mistress (Psalm <b>123</b>:2-3), a woman comforting her baby (Psalm <b>131</b>:2;
Isaiah <b>66</b>:13), a woman giving birth (Deuteronomy<b> 32</b>:18; Isaiah<b>
42</b>:14); a mother taking care of her baby (Hosea<b> 11</b>:3-4), a nursing
mother (Isaiah <b>49</b>:14), a woman looking for a coin (Luke <b>15</b>:8-10),
a mother bear robbed of her cubs (Hosea <b>13</b>:9); a mother eagle
(Deuteronomy <b>32</b>:11-12). Allegorizing these comparisons would be like
claiming there can be female apostles because Paul says he is in travail again
till Christ is formed in you (Galatians <b>4</b>:19).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Look
also at comments above on Genesis <b>1</b>:27 </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew <b>24</b>:14 [time
of Christs return]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>The
gospel of the kingdom must be proclaimed in the whole world </i>[</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>]<i>
as a testimony to all the nations, and then the end will come</i> (= Mark <b>13</b>:10).
The frequent inference is that Christs second coming will not happen until the
gospel reaches every nation. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Jesus
is talking, however, about the end of Jerusalem and the Jewish nation, not the
end of the world or the Christian age. The misreading supposes that <i>world</i>
means the whole world in modern English parlance. Romans <b>1</b>:8;
Colossians <b>1</b>:6, 23; and 1 Timothy <b>3</b>:16 speak of the gospel as
having already been preached throughout the world (</span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>or
</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>).
In Acts <b>24</b>:5 Tertullus says that Paul had caused <i>insurrections among
Jews throughout the world </i>[</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>]<i>.
</i>Acts<b> 11</b>:28 tells about a famine over the whole <i>world</i> (</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>)
in the days of the emperor Claudius. See also perhaps Romans<b> 16</b>:25-26. Such
statements indicate the then-known world, the inhabited world (&lt; </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>,
to dwell), the Roman empire. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
point of Jesus prediction is that the fall of the Jewish state would not occur
until it was clear that his movement was permanent, that it was not just
another fly-by-night Messianic claimant. About a generations time had pretty
well crystallized the number of Jewish believers and unbelievers (Acts <b>5</b>:33-40).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew <b>26</b>:26 [real
presence] [impanation] [transubstantiation] [consubstantiation]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> At
the institution Jesus says, <i>This is my body.</i> The words are pressed to
mean that the bread was actually his body, and the fruit of the vine was
actually his blood. Accordingly, the idea is that when the officiant offers the
prayer of dedication, the essence of the elements changes to the actual flesh
and blood of the incarnate Christ or is intermingled with them.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Nothing
about the Christian message or the efficacy of its operation suggests or requires
such a way of taking the words of institution. His body is holding what he
calls my body. All the features of the elements remain those of the bread and
the fruit of the vine. By appealing to the Greek philosophical distinction
between essence and accidents, an interpreter can claim that the traits of the
elements remain the same while the essence changes. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Obviously,
there is no way to disprove such an idea because essence is not susceptible
to <u>perception</u>. But the real point is that there is no positive basis for
it; the <u>words</u> do not require such an idea, and nothing relevant is
gained by having the bread and the fruit of the vine actually become Christs
flesh and blood. In fact, we no longer know, or relate to, Christ after the
flesh (2 Corinthians <b>5</b>:16). The emblems remind us that as deity he
lived in our physical human circumstance and experienced its most extreme range
of suffering while maintaining his commitment to the Fathers will. We commit
ourselves to that same level of obedience in identifying ourselves with him
through these tokens of his physical existence here. Any transformation
herebread to body, wine to bloodoccurs in the mind of the observer, not in
the elements observed; they are regarded as meaning what they refer to; they
are emblems of those things, not the things themselves in whole or in part. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>When
the Christian message entered the Greek world, one serious difficulty in
interpretation was the tendency to hypostatize itto take its words and
meaning in a hypostatic sense, to interpret them as referring to substance/stuff
rather than to spirit. People readily say of a photograph, <i>This is my
mother, </i>not meaning at all that it is substantively <i>my mother.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>If
Jesus is going to eat this supper with us in the kingdom of heaven, would that
mean eating himself?</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>So
we have here another example of taking the wording of the text in an
unnecessary way and making it orthodox. The hermeneutical method breaks three
rules of interpretation: (1) not staying with minimum affirmation (<i>i.e</i>.,
not over-interpreting, hermeneutical restraint), (2) not reading the unclear in
light of the clear, and (3) not keeping the burden of proof on the affirmative.
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew
<b>27</b>:46 [the Fathers abandonment of the Son]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Eli,
Eli, why have you forsaken me? </i>In keeping with the penal substitution
theory of atonement, the Cry of Dereliction is taken to mean that the Father abandoned
(objectively) the only perfectly righteous Son as he was substituting for the
many sinners. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
gospel account itself offers no explanation for the meaning of this cry from
the cross; so any specific significance attached to it comes from a larger
framework invoked to imply a more specific meaning than is evident from the
words themselves. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>About
the closest thing to objective abandonment is a mans subjective sense of being
alone when dying in agony.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
cry quotes Psalm<b> 22</b>:1, a Messianic Psalm. Jesus may have cried out the
verse to explain to the people and warn the Pharisees themselves about what
they were doing: laughing at him, shooting out the lip, shaking their heads in
ridicule, and saying, <i>He trusted in God; let him now deliver him if he
wants him</i> (Psalm <b>22</b>:7-8 = Matthew<b> 27</b>:39, 42-43). They did
not realize what they were doing (Luke <b>23</b>:34), even though from their
comment about his trusting in God (<b>27</b>:43), they evidently saw the
connection Jesus later made (<b>27</b>:46) between what was happening and the
words of the Psalm. Of course, they just did not believe it was a valid
connection. Jesus point would be that the one crying out to God in the Psalm
was vindicated as Jesus would be as well, not by coming down from the cross,
but by coming up from the grave. God had not departed from the king of the
Jews (<b>23</b>:42) any more than he had abandoned David of old who had
trusted in God and whom Jesus was quoting. Besides, that cry of David had
nothing to do with being alone because he was the substitute for others.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See
also Mark <b>15</b>:22-37; Psalm <b>22</b>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See essays on
Atonement.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew <b>28</b>:19 [proclaim
as you go]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> In
an effort to encourage people to take advantage of daily opportunities to share
Christ as they go about their normal routines, some have slipped into using <i>Go
into all the world</i> as a statement about evangelism as part of the natural
Christian life. Whatever may be said of the idea as such, it is not the point
of this passage. This command is a commissioning of the apostles in their
distinctive work, not a comment to typical people about how to incorporate
evangelism into daily living.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
Greek text combines an aorist participle with an imperative form, which makes
both form imperatival in value. See Places Where Greek Helps. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
broader idea could be better exemplified from the Mission of the Twelve in
Matthew <b>10</b>:5-15, where Jesus uses a present participle tied to a present
imperative when he tells the disciples, <i>As you go, preach, saying . . . </i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Matthew
<b>28</b>:18 [trine baptism]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Trine
baptism performs the baptizing action three times because Father, Son, and
Spirit are all three mentioned.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> It
is worth noting that the formula Jesus uses in his directive to the apostles
is <i>baptizing them into the name of the Father and the son and the Holy
Spirit.</i> The text does not say <i>into the names . . .</i> nor does it
say<i>, into the name of the Father and the name of the Son and the name of
the Holy Spirit.</i> The three share a common identity&quot;into the name,
and their oneness is the larger truth than their distinctness from one another.
While we would not invalidate either Jesus-only baptism or trine baptism, the
bases for requiring either of them does not bear its weight of proof.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Mark <b>6</b>:5-5 [faith
healing]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Jesus
could not do any mighty works in Nazareth because of the peoples unbelief.
Modern faith-healers sometimes try to excuse their failed attempts at faith
healing by saying that the person does not have enough faithsomething that
could be said about anyones faith. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
success of a miracle, however, does not depend on the recipient in a causal
sense as evidenced by Jesus healing Malchus ear in the Garden of Gethsemane
(Luke <b>22</b>:51; John <b>18</b>:10-11). Matthews account of the first
rejection at Nazareth (<b>13</b>:54-58) concludes with the comment, <i>He did
not do many mighty works there because of their unbelief. </i>Not being able
to do many miracles in Nazareth was because he chose to do his miracles in the
context of faith or engendering faith (condition), not that he did not have the
ability to do so unless the recipient had faith. The kingdom of heaven is not
about performing miracles but about accomplishing reconciliation. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Mark<b> 8</b>:29-30 [Messianic
Secret]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>On
at least eight occasions, Jesus told his disciples, those whom he had healed,
and demons not to make him known. In the Good Confession, Peter had just
expressed his confidence that Jesus was the Messiah. This admonition to secrecy
has been misused to say (1) that Jesus himself never claimed to be the Messiah;
it was an exaggerated claim that arose later among his followers (= Matthew<b>
16</b>:20). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Twice
his admonition to silence about healing had the adverse effect (Matthew <b>9</b>:30-31;
Mark <b>7</b>:36). So others have suggested that (2) Jesus was using reverse
psychology to get his name out there all the moresomething he hardly needed
to do. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>A
better explanation lies in the opposite direction: (3) Jesus wanted to keep his
prominence in check till he had time to adjust the prevalent Jewish misconception
that Messiah had come to be a national delivera real concern as shown when his
<u>proponents</u> attempted after the feeding of the five thousand to take him by
force and make him king (John<b> 6</b>:15). He also told his own disciples
not to say he was the Messiah; they did not yet understand either what Messiah
meant (Matthew <b>16</b>:20). Alternatively, <u>opponents</u> of his influence
wanted him out of the picture before a popular uprising overran the national
leadership (cp. later John <b>11</b>:47-53); pushing too publicly too early
would only stymie his already harassed ministry. In a related way, telling
people not to spread news about his healings was meant to restrain excessive
pressure for healing miracles; too much enthusiasm for them would overshadow
his teaching work. In that vein, after a major healing episode in Capernaum, Luke
<b>4</b>:42-44 records that Jesus told the multitudes that wanted him to stay, <i>I
need to preach the gospel to the other cities too.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Furthermore,
the prohibition against advertising him was not absolute. In the case of the
transfiguration, he told the three apostles to delay telling it till after his
resurrection (Matthew <b>17</b>:9 = Mark <b>9</b>:9). More effective, then, was
waiting closer to that ultimate proof of Messiahship before outright claims
were made.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Besides
not encouraging false anticipations about the nature of Messiahs work, Jesus
sought to downplay another type of misinformation: (4) he forbade demons to
testify about him for obvious reasons: their statements would only play into
the hands of religious leaders, who were all too willing to say, <i>Consider
the source.</i> That would give credence to their attempt at explaining his
exorcisms: <i>He casts out demons by Beelzebub</i> (Mark <b>1</b>:34 = Luke <b>4</b>:41;
Matthew <b>12</b>:15-21 = Mark <b>3</b>:12; see Matthew<b> 12</b>:22-37). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Matthew Mark Luke Addressees</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <b>1</b>:34 <b>4</b>:41 demons</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>8</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:4 <b>1</b>:44 a
leper</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>12</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:15-21 <b>3</b>:12 the
healed, demons</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <b>5</b>:43 <b>8</b>:56 Jairus
household</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>9</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:30-31 2
healed blind men</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <b>7</b>:36 people
of the</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Decapolis </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>16</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:20 <b>8</b>:29-30 <b>9</b>:2 the
apostles </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>17</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:9 <b>9</b>:9 the
3 apostles</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Mark <b>10</b>:13-16 (=
Matthew <b>19</b>:13-15 = Luke <b>18</b>:15-17) [infant baptism]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Let
the little children come to me. Of such is the kingdom of heaven. The
kingdom of heaven belongs to such.</i> Receiving the kingdom as a little child
is taken to mean when rather than in the manner of a little child. The
comment cannot mean in the state, or when of a little child; otherwise,
adults would be eliminated since the explanation continues by saying that a
person will not enter it otherwise. So, aside from in the manner of, one
wonders what an infants receiving the kingdom would mean. The infant would
not know that he was, in fact, receiving it.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Cp. Matthew <b>18</b>:1-6;
Mark <b>9</b>:36-37</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Mark <b>10</b>:6 [fiat
creationism]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See comments
under Matthew <b>19</b>:8.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Mark <b>16</b>:18 [snake
handling]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Picking
up serpents need not mean deliberately picking them up any more than drinking
something poisonous would mean deliberately doing so. It is interesting that snaking
handling cults do not venture into the poison drinking too. The two issues are
alike in that they involve poison. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
nature and purpose of the kingdom does not foster showing off supernatural
powers. Jesus promise is better illustrated in Pauls being bitten by a
poisonous snake after the shipwreck on Mileta on the way to Rome. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Putting
ourselves in harms way sounds like putting to trial the Lord our God (Matthew <b>4</b>:6-7
&lt; Deuteronomy <b>6</b>:16), something neither Jesus nor his disciples were
willing to do.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Mark <b>16</b>:17
[permanence of tongue speaking] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>These
signs will accompany believers.</i> The words apply to the time frame the
writer has in mind. <b>16</b>:20 states a fulfillment of the prediction;
anything more goes beyond the scope of the passage. Besides, being able to
speak in tongues is not a matter of choice, but of enablement.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Mark <b>16</b>:17 [necessity
of tongue speaking]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Jesus
includes speaking in new languages in his list of signs that will accompany
believers. He does not say every believer will do so or that such speaking will
continue beyond the time frame marked by the statement of fulfillment in the
subsequent text (<b>16</b>:20). The purpose of speaking in languages lay in
connection with the preaching of the gospel (<b>16</b>:20; cp. 1 Corinthians <b>14</b>),
not as a sign <u>to</u> believers, but <u>to</u> unbelievers (1 Corinthians <b>14</b>:22).
A sign to believers that they are in fact saved, for example, is not what is
needed; faith in Gods promise to that effect is sufficient. A sign to
unbelievers may be useful, but not necessary, because the gospel addresses the
secrets of peoples hearts, and they can thereby perceive that God is in the
proclaimers of the good news (1 Corinthians <b>14</b>:24-25).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Luke<b> 2</b>:1-2 [taxation
at Jesus birth]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> To
be taxed should read to be enrolled. The emperor had ordered a census, which
took place at the time Luke indicates by his list of rulers.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Luke
<b>11</b>:42 [Christians need to practice tithing.]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See
notations on Matthew <b>23</b>:23.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Luke <b>15</b>:11-32
[eternal security]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> While
the prodigal was in the far country, he was still his fathers son; so a
convert who reverts to his former life is still saved as to status. The
prodigal lost the benefits of home and his testimony to others, but he was
still a son. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>However,
the prodigal was only his fathers physical son, not his spiritual one. The
father later tells the older brother that, while in the foreign country, the
prodigal was lost as well as deadthough he was physically alive, of
course, but not in a way pertinent to the parable (<b>15</b>:32). Anything
about physical sonship lies outside the point of the parable; so nothing in
spiritual sonship corresponds to something in salvationa least as could be
shown from this parable. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Salvation
is reconciliation from alienationan interpersonal process and condition (2 Corinthians
<b>5</b>:18-19; Romans <b>5</b>:10-11; Ephesians <b>2</b>:12-16; Colossians <b>1</b>:19-20;
<i>e.g</i>.); physical connection, legal status, logical structure do not characterize
the concern; such things are foreign to the enterprise. Salvation is a present
relationship, not an impersonal state called heaven, or a place of <i>not
being in hell</i> or <i>saved from going to hell </i>in the future</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Luke
<b>17</b>:34 [acceptance of homosexual behavior]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> At
the time of the end, Jesus says two will be on one bed, one taken and the other
left. It is a stretch to suppose that they were in the same bed because they typically
had homosexual relations. That would be like trying to justify pedophile
behavior because Jesus tells a parable about a man being in bed with his
children (Luke<b> 11</b>:7). They can be in bed together for other than homosexual
sexual relations. Ecclesiastes<b> 4</b>:11 says, for example, <i>If two lie
down together, they are warm; how can one be warm alone?</i> The clear
prohibition against homosexual relations eliminates an unnecessary take on any
text elsewhere: Romans<b> 1</b>:26-27.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> An
aside observation may be of interest. A reader expects Luke <b>17</b>:34 to mean
two men, but there is no word for men; that is an inference based on a
masculine article. Jesus offers a parallel case where two are grinding
together, one taken and the other left (<b>17</b>:35); again, the noun <i>women</i>
does not appear in the Greek text. Men and women in parallel make the best
sense between the two sets since women did the grinding. The subsequent uses of
the one in both comparisons are two masculine articles in the first case and
then two feminine ones in the second case. The subsequent masculine articles and
feminine articles would be carrying out the masculine-takes-preference usage at
the first part of the comments. The word two (</span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>)
itself, however, has the same form for masculine and feminine, and mixed
reference by custom uses masculine form.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Luke
<b>18</b>:8 [Will orthodox faith be found on earth when Christ returns?]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
article plus noun <i>the faith</i> is taken to mean that Jesus wonders whether
the true faith will exist on earth when he returns. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>But
will he find the faith or will he find faith. The Greek article on the noun
does not have to mean the body of doctrine so as to imply that doctrinal
accuracy is the point of Jesus question. The latter emphasis is sometimes
taken as a basis for urging some kind of orthodoxy the current speaker wants
to promote to the exclusion of other false doctrinal faiths on the current
scene. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>It
is better taken <i>faith</i> as a reference to the kind of thing faith is. <i>Will
faith be found on the earth? Will there be people fill with faith/trust in
God?</i> The Lords comment is made after the Parable of the Unjust Judge, in
which Jesus encourages people to pray, <u>trusting</u> that God will answer and
give them justice. Will Christ find that kind of trust/faith when he returns?
The Greek article marks contrast with parallel alternatives. That value can be
one individual within a group (definiteness) or it can mark one group in
contrast to another, in this case faith in contrast to the lack of it.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Luke<b> 18</b>:17 [infant
baptism]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Let
the little children come to me; dont forbid them, for of such is the kingdom
of heaven.</i> Jesus words are taken by extension to imply the
appropriateness of infant baptismsince they are then in the kingdom and those
in the kingdom are baptized into it.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> One
typical counter-response has limited application: <i><u>Let them come</u> is a
different thing from <u>bringing them</u> (to baptism).</i> It is not clear that
the passages record an incident just about ambulatory children (cp. Matthew <b>19</b>:13-15;
Mark <b>10</b>:13-16).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> More to the
point is that the text is not dealing with baptism or salvation from lostness. Without
some prior indication of the relevance of baptism to infants, the text offers
nothing to aid in that identification. Instead, it highlights the typical attitude
of little children, who trust their parents even when they do not understand
why they do what they do or require of them what they require. That trusting
attitude pictures peoples appropriate attitude toward God and satisfies the
intent of Jesus comment. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>If
the passage is taken to imply something about baptism because it connects
children with the kingdom of God, then certain comments seem in order. It is
one thing to say that baptism (identifies us with Christ and on that basis) is
associated with forgiveness of sins; it is another thing to say that without it
there is no safeness, no forgiveness. It is one thing to say that baptism
identifies a person with the kingdom of God; it is another thing to say that
only those baptized does God identify with the kingdom. The lack of infant baptism
does not mean there is no theology of the child in an interpreters
understanding of childrens relationship to God. Aside from irrelevant
considerations, there is no reason to suppose that little ones are lost or that
they are of no import in the concerns of the gospel and the point of salvation.
If <i>such is the kingdom of heaven,</i> then there is no need for them to be
saved into it; they are not lost, and they are already in the kingdom.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Not entering
the kingdom as a little child does not mean when a little child, or no
adult could be saved. The closing verse of Luke <b>18</b>:15-17 says, <i>Whoever
does not receive the kingdom of God as a little child will certainly not enter
it.</i> As means manner, not agein modern parlance, come like a little
child.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>John <b>1</b>:1 [trinity]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>In
the beginning was the Word and the Word was with God and the Word was God.</i>
The Word is somehow thought of as being with God and at the same time being
God. It is as if there were two brothers, Bill and Bob; and somehow Bill was <u>with</u>
Bob and <u>was</u> Bob.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> In
scripture, God covers the general reference to God as (a) godhead and at
other times more specifically (b) God the Father, as when Jesus calls God his
Father (John <b>5</b>:18) and the Jews accuse him of blasphemy for doing so. A
third usage is (c) deity. In this last instance, the stress falls on <u>kind</u>
of being, deity rather than angel, mankind, animal, or the material universe. It
is in this sense that we are to understand John <b>1</b>:1. The Word that
became flesh was previously with God (the Father) and was himself deity. He is
not the same one as the Father (<u>identity</u>)under, say, a different name
(monarchianism) or operating in a second role (economic trinity). He is of the
same <u>kind</u>. In this very gospel,<b> 14</b>:16 makes clear distinction
between the Father and the Son (and the Spirit as well): <i>I will pray to the
Father, and he will send another Comforter.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> In
keeping with the distinction between identity and kind, the original language omits
the article on God when it says. <i>God the Word was.</i> The absence of
the Greek article calls attention to the kind of thing the noun refers to,
hence, deity. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See Places
Where Greek Helps at John<b> 1</b>:1.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>John <b>1</b>:1 [deity]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Some
have noticed that the Greek article does not appear with God in the clause,<i>
The Word was God </i>(or, more literally, <i>and God the word was.</i>). Supposing
that the article in Greek has the same value as the definite article in
English, these readers want to say, <i>And the Word was a god.</i> From that
point the reasoning can going different ways, one being that the Word was God
in the sense that he operated in a role associated with Godfunctional deity. The
idea is somewhat akin to the usage of gods in Psalm <b>82</b>:6, <i>I said,
You are gods,</i> said of those to whom the word of God came, the judges of
Israel (see John<b> 10</b>:34-36).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
problem comes from misunderstanding the value of the Greek article. It does not
equate to the English definite article the so that its absence would mean a
(indefinite). Instead, it means identify <i>vs</i>. quality, or to put it
differently, the article draws attention to something in contrast to one or
more parallel alternatives. The absence of the article, then, draws attention
to the kind of thing it is as thought of on its own terms rather than in
contrast to others in the category.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> In
both this entry and the last one, the strained interpretations come from the
underlying assumption that one God (monotheism) means a single individual
person rather than an <u>interpersonal oneness</u> having the <u>same</u>
nature and <u>united</u> purpose and being the <u>only</u> entity of that kind.
The belief stems from a fear of polytheism or tritheism (three-god-ism), an
issue dealt with elsewhere in this website under The Doctrine of God. The
closest thing to the threeness-in-oneness of deity is the twoness-in-oneness of
husband-wife (minus, of course, the physical dimension), on the one hand, as
distinguished from a man and a woman, on the other.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See Places
Where Greek Helps at John <b>1</b>:1.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>John <b>1</b>:9
[universalism]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i></i>[Jesus]
<i>was the true light that enlightens every person that comes into the world.</i>
Consequently, at some point, either during life or later in eternity, all persons
will eventually be saved (enlightened).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>John
refers to Christs role for all people, his value, his potential. That is the
character of what he brought into the world and who he was; that was his motive
for comingto bring salvation to all people. Because of what this salvation
is and who this savior is, the enlightening happens only when people respond to
the light. It is interpersonal, requiring both offering, on the one side, and accepting,
on the other side.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> This text
cannot mean that all people are saved, as universalism believes. John proceeds
to say that not everyone received him. A difference exists between the <u>all</u>
Christ came to the world to enlighten and the <u>less than all</u> he gave the
right to become Gods children because they received him. The difference
between the two numbers lies in people who did not not receive him.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>John <b>1</b>:19 [anti-Semitism]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Critics
sometimes accuse New Testament writers of anti-Semitism. Part of the basis for
the idea comes from the negative use of the Jews, particularly in Johns
gospel and in The Acts.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> One
corrective on this understanding is that (a) the Jews does not refer to Jews in
general, but to resistant and hostile Jews, those who opposed Christ and
persecuted his disciples. That opposition primarily came from the Jewish
national and religious leaders during Christs ministry. Many Jews became
Christians, and so do not fall under the use of Jews in an adversative sense.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Furthermore,
(b) the Jews does not always carry a pejorative sense. In John about
one-third of the uses have neutral connotations.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>John
and Acts were written from a later perspectivethe 90s (?) and the 60s
respectively, when the number of Jewish converts to Christ was pretty well
settled; the influence of the religious leaders and traditional expectancies
about the Messianic kingdom had crystalized the Jew-Christian distinction. That
process made the terminology all the more appropriate, because more generally the
Jews amounted to non-Christians of Jewish heritage. So Jews takes its place
alongside Gentiles, Samaritans, Romans, Greeks, and the like as a
designation that no more bears a negative sense than those other terms do. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Even
then, in the earlier stages of this divergence, (c) Acts takes opportunity to
note the favorable attitude that the general populace took toward the converts
(<b>2</b>:47a; <b>4</b>:21; <b>5</b>:13, 26). Similarly, there is a note that
not all the leaders evidenced hostility toward the Christian movement: <i>A
large company of the priests obeyed faith/the faith</i> (Acts <b>6</b>:7). Special
mention is made of Nicodemus (John <b>3</b>:1-12ff.; <b>7</b>:50-52; <b>19</b>:39)
and Joseph of Arimathea (John <b>19</b>:38 = Matthew <b>27</b>:57-61 + Mark <b>15</b>:42-47
= Luke<b> 23</b>:50-56) even during the ministry of Jesus. Acts records that
even Gamaliel, the apostle Pauls famous teacher (Acts <b>22</b>:3),
successfully cautioned the Sanhedrin against dealing ruthlessly with Christian
leaders (Acts <b>5</b>:33-40). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Lastly,
we find (d) several gracious comments toward the Jews who rejected Jesus as their
Messiah: the Lords cry from the cross, <i>Father, forgive them; they dont
know what theyre doing </i>(Luke <b>23</b>:34? ms); likewise, Stephens amazing,
dying prayer, <i>Lord, dont hold this sin against them</i> (Acts <b>7</b>:60).
Peter tells an assembly of Jews in Solomons Porch, <i>Brothers, I know you
did it </i>[crucified Jesus] <i>in ignorance, as your rulers did too </i>(Acts
<b>3</b>:17).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Given
the atrocities carried out against Christs followers by many leaders and
members of the Jewish community, rather than anti-Semitism, the more notable
observation is the lack of vitriol against opponents of the Messianic movement.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>John <b>2</b>:15 [abuse]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> In
Johns account of the cleansing of the temple (<b>2</b>:13-22), Jesus made a
lash/scourge/whip (</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>)
and supposedly used it on the merchants and money changers to drive them out of
the temple area.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> More exactly,
the text says, <i>Having made a whip out of ropes, he drove all [</i></span><i><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span></i><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>]
out of the templeboth the sheep and the oxen . . . ,</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
and told the ones selling doves to get them out of the temple. <i>Both the
sheep and the oxen</i> renames (appositives) what all refers to; so the
account does not say that Jesus used it on the people, but on the animals. In
fact, the merchants are still around to call for something to show that he had
the authority to do what he did (<b>2</b>:18-20).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>John <b>3</b>:7 [salvation
as supernatural]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Jesus
tells Nicodemus that a person has to be born again, or perhaps born from
above. That image has been taken to indicate something supernatural because it
is not physical.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
expression born again need not mean more than starting over in a new frame of
reference that is not physical birth (Matthew <b>3</b>:8-9, <i>e.g</i>.),
starting over in a transcendent, spiritual, interpersonal way. Spiritual
need not mean supernatural; it simply means not physical, as Nicodemus had
supposed Jesus must have meant. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>What
happens in conversion must be learned from descriptions elsewhere that more
clearly address the nature of the process than a single word image can do. From
the rest of scripture, we learn that the issue in salvation is reconciling
alienated persons by the factors always involved in reconciling (2 Corinthians<b>
5</b>:18-19; Romans <b>5</b>:10-11; Ephesians<b> 2</b>:12-16; Colossians<b> 1</b>:19-20,
<i>e.g</i>.). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Bringing
divine supernatural activity into the process arises from the prior belief that
everybody has biologically inherited from our first parents a fallen nature
that has to be regenerated supernaturally before conviction, conversion, and
continued Christian living can take place. If that idea does not belong in the
picture, introducing a supernatural element into the new birth word picture
does not commend itself. Natural depravity (<i>vs</i>. behavioral depravity)
has to be dealt with from other texts and in response to texts appealed to.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Regarding
natural depravity, see TOPICS &gt; anthropology &gt; depravity.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>John<b> 5</b>:24
[Christians and judgment]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>The
one that hears my word and believes him that sent me, has entered into eternal
life and does not come into judgment, but has passed out of death into life</i>.
Does not come into judgment has been taken to mean that Christians does not
go through the final judgment indicated in Revelation and elsewhere.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The word
translated judgment has a meaning wide enough to include condemnation, a
negative outcome of judging. The latter fits the Great White Throne Judgment pictured
in Revelation <b>20</b>:11-15. That prophecy speaks of everyone appearing at
the event, and anyone not found in the Book of Life was cast into the lake of
fire. Similarly, in The Olivet Discourse, Jesus pictures the Son of man sitting
on his throne with all the nations gathered in front of him, and he separates
the sheep from the goats and sends the two groups on their separate ways; <i>every
man was judged</i> (Matthew <b>25</b>:31-46). So, Christians are
evaluated-judged just like everyone else, but they are not condemned-judged because
they have put their trust in Christs righteousness.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>John <b>6</b>:37
[irresistible grace]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:6.0pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>All
that the Father gives me will come to me. </i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:6.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
emphasis is on <i>Father</i>, not <i>give</i>. Jesus is contrasting <u>Father</u>
and <u>people</u>. People that do not come were not given by the Father. The
Father gives in that <u>he</u> sets the norms and terms of acceptably coming to
the intended Messiah, not what <u>people</u> supposed the Messiah would be and
do (cp. John<b> 6</b>:44). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>John<b> 6</b>:44
[irresistible grace]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>No
one can come to me unless the Father . . . draws him.</i> By emphasizing <u>draws</u>,
the Lords comment comes to mean that people cannot come to Christ if the
Father does <u>not</u> draw them. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
import, however, is that people cannot be coming to Jesus as Messiah unless the
<u>Father</u> is the one drawing themin contrast to their own or other
peoples ideas drawing them. The comment reminds us of Jesus statement to Peter
that his confession <i>did not come from flesh and blood but from the Father
that is in heaven</i> (Matthew <b>16</b>:15). Jesus did not fit the religious
leaders expectancies; instead, he was exhibiting the Fathers real intensions;
so they were not drawn to him. Misemphasis in reading creates the wrong
contrast.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>John<b> 8</b>:7
[criticism] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> In
the episode about the woman taken in adultery, Jesus says, <i>Let the one
without sin among you, cast the first stone.</i> The comment is used to fend
off anyone who objects to a certain persons sin or takes actions appropriate
to it. The wording becomes an excuse for not owning up to ones own sin by
shifting attention to the accuser. If that were its import, the church could
not exercise church discipline (Matthew <b>18</b>:15-20). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Jesus
comment does apply to a condemnatory attitude toward someone guilty of significant
sin. It is well-taken as an admonition in respect to the <u>person</u> who
sins, but it should not be stretched to deflect objection to the persons <u>act</u>as
if no one else has any right to say that what someone is doing is morally
wrong. In such cases, the counter motive would be to stifle moral standards. The
difference here is the same as that between hate speech (against a person)
and identifying certain acts as sin (behavior). The difference likewise applies
to disagreeing with, say, a Muslims beliefs (concepts) about God and blasphemy
(degrading attitude) against his God. In the last case, the motive is to
justify retaliation, holy war, and the like.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
verse stands in a text whose integrity has been questioned on the basis of
manuscript evidence. That point does not seem to affect the discussion on the
idea as such.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See
comments on Matthew <b>7</b>:1.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See also Passages
Where Greek Helps.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>John <b>10</b>:26-29
[eternal security]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>No
one will snatch you out of my hand. </i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>No
