Places Where Greek Helps at this place.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Cp. <i>What
the Bible Says About Salvation</i>, pp. 122-23.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Romans <b>11</b>:29 [divine
immutability/unchangeableness/irreversible salvation/universalism/ </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>effectual
calling]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>The
gifts and calling of God are not repented of.</i> The passage has been taken
to indicate that once people are saved, God does not reverse the decision to
save them regardless of whether they continue in faith and practice.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Getting
such a meaning from the words requires taking them as a general principle, but Paul
cannot be laying down a general principle of divine operation here. That would
contradict examples of withdrawing gifts and cases of Gods repenting. The
translation can be worded to fit Pauls present topic: <i>The gifts and
calling of God are not being repented of.</i> Translating the verb as present
progressive rather than characteristic present refers to the present discussion
rather than stating a universal principle. The present discussion about
including Gentiles as Gentiles in the gospel does not mean God has revoked
Israels calling as the nation through whom all the peoples of the earth would
be blessed. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
salvation <u>possibility</u> has not been removed from Israel. The salvation <u>actuality</u>
has not become true for Israel as whole; only a remnant of any people at any
time will become Gods people (<b>11</b>:6).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Again,
we say that Gods never changing his gifts and calling does not fit with the
rest of scripture. God called Saul and his dynasty to be the royal house of
Israel, but he later withdrew that calling because Saul disobeyed (1 Samuel <b>16</b>:1-13;
2 Samuel<b> 7</b>:12-16). He repented of his intention to destroy Nineveh because
the city repented. The threat was a conditional one. Gods gifts and callings
therefore may be conditional; so when the conditions are not met, God does not
give the results, or he withdraws results already in part bestowed when ongoing
conditions cease to be met. In the case of choosing a nation through which to
bless the others, the gifts and calling were not conditioned on the continued
faithfulness of Israel; their chosen status stood on the basis of Gods love (regards)
for the fathers (<b>11</b>:28). So Paul does not state a general method of
divine operation herea divine operation that can then be transferred to other
topics; he makes a claim regarding the choice (election) of Israel as
caretaker of salvation history.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> At a more
general level, some qualification must be made on the idea that Gods decisions
are alterable, or he would be locked into all previous decisions about how to
do things with other persons.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Topic 2: [effectual
calling to personal salvation/irresistible grace]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Pauls
statement cannot be a general principle of divine operation (see above). It
cannot be extended, then, beyond choosing Israel as the <u>curator of the
message</u> about relating to the one true God. An individuals acceptance of, and
benefit from, that message is a different topic from Israels being the
caretaker of that message. Therefore, irresistible (effectual) calling to an individuals
personal salvation finds no foundation in the words of this verse.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See
Places Where Greek Helps at this place.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Cp. <i>What
the Bible Says About Salvation</i>, pp. 189-91, 474, 478-80.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Romans <b>12</b>:1 [worship]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Present
your bodies as living sacrifices . . . [which is] your reasonable service.</i>
Since some translations render the last word as worship, readers have used this
text to define worship. The term in the original, however (</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>),
means religious service more generally, which makes it too broad for worship
in a specific sense. Texts that use worship to translate </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
(word picture, bend the knee toward) provide a clearer idea distinguishable
from general Christian living: service to God as present, superior, second
person. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See <i>Basic
Christianity</i>: Worship.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Romans <b>16</b>:2 [egalitarianism]
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> To
the Romans Paul commends Phoebe, a servant (</span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>)
of the church in Cenchrea. Some interpreters have fixed on the word picture in
the term translated helper. It is compound of <i>pro</i> (before, for) and <i>stand</i>,
a combination then taken to mean one who stands in front of, hence, a leader.
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>However,
<i>She has been a ____ of many, and of myself.</i> She would not have been a
leader of Paul; so the standard translation helper fits the situation better.
The word picture of standing in front of connects with helping in that such a
person is pictured as standing at hand, <i>i.e</i>., ready to help.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Romans <b>16</b>:7 [egalitarianism]
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Andronicus
and Junias, my kinsmen </i>were of note among the apostles. In the original, <i>Junias</i>
is in the direct object form <i>Junian</i>, which could be the accusative form
either for <i>Junias</i>, a mans name, or <i>Junia</i>, a womans name. So gender
is indeterminate in this case. The two names could represent a husband-wife
couple like Aquila and Priscilla, or two men related to Paul (spiritually?).
The accompanying my kinsmen is masculine; but that would not disallow the
second name of the pair to be feminine, since classical languages use masculine
gender for mixed situations. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>With
the gender issue unclear, the attempt to use the reference to say that Junia
was a notable woman among the apostles is indeterminate and unwarranted. Furthermore,
<i>being of note among the apostles</i> does not necessarily mean the two
were apostles positionally in some leadership sense; it may mean only that the
apostles considered them noteworthyapostles associationally. Passages like
Romans<b> 16</b>:2, 7 taken to reinforce the belief that men and women are
completely interchangeable in Christ illustrate failure to take the unclear in
light of the clear.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Corinthians <b>1</b>:12
[the Christ party]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Paul
lists four groups of Corinthian Christians: people that identified with Paul,
with Apollos, with Cephas, and with Christ. The question comes as to what the
Christ party consisted of. Paul concludes his discussion of division in
Corinth by saving in <b>3</b>:23 that they actually do belong to Christ, who
belongs to God.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Some
readers what to emphasize that last comment and bring it back to apply to the
phraseology in<b> 1</b>:12, including the admonition that Christs body should
not be separated up into denominations, as something of an indictment on the
current situation in Christendom. In response to that warning, others have
claimed that the Christ party was worst of all because they saw themselves as
the true followers of the Lord. Of course, trying to ferret out the nature of
that fourth part is not possible at this late date. It remains best then to
apply the hermeneutical principle of taking the unclear in light of the clear
or leaving the unclear unspecified. In this case, we should all recognize the
clear principle the apostle states in <b>3</b>:23: we belong to Christ. That
has to be the proper motive and attitude toward Christ and each other
regardless of how well we can bring that mentality into reality. In doing so,
we certainly do not want to create yet another denomination in an effort to get
rid of denominationalism!</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Other
identifications of the Christ party with some sort of Gnostic sect are a second
line of inquiry that has too little to go on to amount to anything fruitful in
New Testament study.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Corinthians<b> 1</b>:17
[downplay baptism] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> If the
wording <i>Christ did not send me to baptize </i>were taken to mean it is not
part of the response to the proclaimed message, the apostle would be speaking
at odds with the Great Commission. Paul is talking about performing baptisms,
not about including baptism in the conversion process. His remark comes in a context
where he considers it a matter of good fortune that he did not perform more
baptisms in Corinth than he did; it might have fostered the notion that, so to
speak, he was baptizing people to himself, a matter that would have falsely
reinforced the tendency for Corinthians to identify themselves with one or
another of Christs ministers.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Corinthians <b>1</b>:21
[the preaching ministry]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>It
pleased God by the foolishness of preaching to save them that believe. </i>This
King James rendering has often served as a basis for encouraging men to go into
the preaching ministryto give themselves over to what the world would consider
a waste of time, talent, and treasure.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
text should read <i>the thing preached</i>; it serves as part of Pauls
contrast between human wisdom and rhetorical eloquence, on the one hand, and
power of the message through Christ to speak to the inner yearnings of the
heart (cp. <b>14</b>:25). The contrast is between form (eloquence) and content
(message) as well as between content (from human wisdom) and content (from
divine revelation) rather than between ways of disseminating it. The
foolishness of preaching application to ministry is an example of a decent
idea in itself that is then misusing a certain text as a basis for it.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See Greek
Helps here.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Corinthians <b>2</b>:14
[natural depravity]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>A
natural man does not receive [</span></i><i><span lang=EL style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span></i><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>,
welcome?] the things of the Spirit of God because they are spiritually
discerned.</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The passage, put in the service of inherited
natural depravity, is thought to mean that a person descended from Adam does
not have the ability to accept spiritual truth. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:6.0pt;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Such
a take on the wording might be possible were natural depravity an already-established
viewpoint. As it is, Pauls intent of meaning is that people committed to (<b>2</b>:16)
carnal things (<b>3</b>:3) do not accept spiritual things, not because they
have no ontic ability to do so, but because they have no circumstantial
ability to do so. Operating in one system, they are predisposed not to welcome
understandings incompatible with that system. Being oriented to, interested in,
committed to the earthly, material, physical, tangible realm, they lack a
propensity toward spiritual things; Gods concerns are foreign to that frame of
mind. Spiritual things have to be appreciated from a spiritual frame of
reference (cp. <b>1</b>:19). Wisdom, eloquence, and the like (<b>1</b>:18-<b>2</b>:5)
appeal to a different mentality than the one concerned with personal matters
with God and other people. (Note that the context deals with division among the
Corinthians.) </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Note:
An illustration might help distinguish ontic <i>vs</i>. circumstantial
inability. A world-class athlete cannot run a hundred-yard dash in a jail cell.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Corinthians <b>6</b>:12
[non-morality of all acts]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>All
things are lawful for me, but not all things are expedient.</i> Evidently the
point is that not everything that is lawful is expedient or edifying. All
equals a lot of things (are lawful); it is a significant generality, not an
absolute uniformity. Other cases exist where Hebrew </span><span lang=HE
dir=RTL style='font-size:14.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
dir=LTR></span><span lang=HE style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'><span dir=LTR></span> </span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>and
Greek<span dir=RTL></span><span dir=RTL><span dir=RTL></span> </span></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>mean
everything or everybody in a phenomenological sense. <i>All the people said</i>
means everybody (so it seemed) said. Cases include Mark<b> 1</b>:5; John<b> 12</b>:19.
See instances referenced in <i>InterpretationGetting the Point</i> notes where
all is limited.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See also 1
Corinthians <b>10</b>:23.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Corinthians <b>7</b>:25-35
[celibacy] [clerical celibacy]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Pauls
concern about marriage and the ministry was not tainted by the pagan notion
that matter is somehow evil or less excellent. Rather, he wanted to remove a
snare to his readers (<b>7</b>:35). He had practical reasons in mind, not natur-al
(ontic) ones. He worried that prospective persecution would make life and
ministry more difficult for married people (<b>7</b>:28-29). He understood, of
course, that, all things being otherwise equal, ministry can be done with
greater diligence without the responsibilities of marriage and family. Paul
said elsewhere that he had the right to have a wife like the rest of the
apostles, the Lords brothers, and Peter (1 Corinthians <b>9</b>:5; Mark <b>1</b>:30).
In the other direction, however, under normal circumstances, many aspects of
ministry are carried on more effectively by people who have gained credibility
by good marriage and family life.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Turning
Pauls advice into commandment for ministers presses the issue into a rigidness
that does not fit well with the way most people are built (Matthew <b>19</b>:10-12;
Genesis <b>2</b>:18). What is circumstantial should not be mandated as
universal.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:6.0pt;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See
1 Corinthians <b>7</b>: 7-9; cp. Luke <b>21</b>:23</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Corinthians <b>9</b>:27
[self-flagellation] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>I
buffet my body and bring it into bondage.</i> There is no reason to associate
Pauls words with the later practice of literally beating ones body with whips
to bring the spirit and will under subjection (self-flagellation). The imagery
reflects the preceding text that uses the Greek games to picture deliberate,
relevant exercise for physical training. It is reminiscent of calling Pauls
thorn in the flesh, a messenger from Satan to buffet him lest he become
proud. It is difficult to see how physical self-abuse would foster spiritual sanctity.
</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Abusing
the body does not purify the soul. <i>Severity to the body . . . is of no
value against the indulgence of the flesh </i>(Colossians <b>2</b>:23). Extreme
fasting, celibacy, withdrawal from social relations, sleep deprivation, and
literal flagellation reflect the depreciation of the material realm derived
from Iranian, Greek, gnostic dualism. Such practices disdained the flesh, and in
many cases attempted to escape from it as part of the quest to become pure
spirit. That is not the perspective of biblical revelation, which from the
beginning pronounced the material world good (Genesis <b>1</b>:31, <i>etc</i>.).
The programmatic in the Judaeo-Christian faith is escape from alienation, not
escape from material existence. It concerns itself with interpersonal behavior,
not with metaphysical knowledge on how to escape our present form of existence.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> In I Timothy <b>4</b>:8
Paul makes the statement that bodily exercise profits little/a little. That
sentiment seems to look in the opposite direction from the idea behind self-flagellation.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Corinthians <b>11</b>:22,
34 [prohibition of church dinners]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Censuring
the Corinthians, Paul says, <i>What, dont you have houses to eat in? If
anyone is hungry, let them eat at home. </i>So, churches should not have communal
meals, or church dinners? </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The apostles
comments stand among correctives on the way (some of) his readers were
conducting themselves in the Corinthians agape feasts. His point is that if
they are going to operate in their inconsiderate ways as a group, eat at home
individually. That is different from requiring them to eat at home. There is
nothing about eating together that makes it wrong to do; so the prohibition is
not against doing it, but against doing it inappropriately. What is
circumstantial should not be mandated as universal.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Corinthians <b>11</b>:27
[unworthiness to observe communion] [communion observance]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Whoever
eats the bread and drinks the cup of the Lord unworthily</i> is guilty of the
body and blood of the Lord. The apostle is talking about manner (adverb) of
observance, not the character (adjective) of the observer: whoever drinks in
an unworthy manner. Naturally, Christian character always connects with all
acts of worship and human relations, but that is a different thing from avoiding
worship because no person could consider himself worthy. Paul himself could not
have partaken (Philippians<b> 3</b>:12-13).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
spirit of Pauls admonition stands against perfunctory, superficial, ritualistic,
formal observanceany activity that does not attach appropriate meaning of the
emblems (<b>11</b>:29-32). Besides, in the context the apostle denounces their communion
services because of the divisions and insensitivity that had perverted these
agape feasts into overeating by some in front of others left hungry (<b>11</b>:17-22
+ 33-34). Under such circumstances <i>it is not possible to eat the Lords
Supper</i> (<b>11</b>:20); such communicants are observing the memorial meal
unworthily. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> About the
closest thing to an unworthy participant might be someone who has sinned against
a brother. In a worship act involving sacrifice, Jesus said to straighten
things out with the brother before offering the gift to God. Insofar as
communion might parallel sacrificing as an act of worship, the pattern could
suggest not coming before God without first trying to fix the human alienation
(Matthew <b>5</b>:23-24).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Corinthians<b> 13</b>:1
[glossolalia]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Tongues of
angels is an exaggeration for emphasis in somewhat the same vein as Pauls
statement to the Galatians, <i>Though an angel from heaven should preach to
you any gospel other than what we preached to you, let him be anathema</i>
(Galatians<b> 1</b>:8). Angels are spirits, and spirits do not have flesh and
bones (Hebrews <b>1</b>:7, 14 + Luke <b>24</b>:39). Consequently, their
communication is not by physical means that could be reproduced like human
languages. As a result, there is not a second, supernatural type of tongues/language
speaking that is different from human languages.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Corinthians <b>13</b>:10
[duration of miraculous gifts]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>When
the perfect has come, the in-part will be done away. </i>In one direction, (a)
the perfect is not Christ at the second coming (perfect is in the wrong
grammatical gender) or that event when he comes. The passage is not useful in
showing that the charismata will last throughout the church age till Christ
comes.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> In
the opposite direction, (b) the perfect also does not refer to the completed
New Testament canon with the implication that when the written word was
completed, miraculous manifestations ceased. Paul is in both the in-part and
perfect situations. He died before the whole New Testament was written.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The reference
is to (c) the next stage of our existence, when we are in face-to-face
relationship with Christ. The stage begins when death takes us into that next
situation; hence, it is already here for those who have gone before. The
contrast is between two states of being, stagers of being, two times of being.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Corinthians <b>13</b>:11
[character of glossolalia]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>When I
was a child I, I talked like a child . . . When I became an adult, I put away
childish things.</i> Whatever a reader may think about present-day tongue
speaking, Paul does not call the phenomenon childish. He is talking about things
that belong to childhood, which better grabs the import of the expression.
Childish carries a negative connotation in English. There is no need to
attach a negative connotation to what the apostle says about this gift. It was
not a childish thing when the apostles used it on Pentecost and other occasions.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Corinthians <b>14</b>:1-4,
14 [Tongue speaking is addressed to God.] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>He that
speaks in a tongue speaks, not to men, but to God, because no one understands.</i>
Paul means that untranslated languages <u>in effect</u> speak to God <i>because
no one understands them.</i> They do so by default, not by intent. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Corinthians<b> 14</b>:(2),
14 [a tongues speaker does not understand what he is saying]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
text says that the tongue speakers understanding is unfruitful. Paul does
not mean tongue speakers have no idea what they are saying; otherwise, he could
not go on to say, <i>You are giving thanks, all right,</i> but other people
cannot say <i>Amen</i> to it (<b>14</b>:17). The speaker understands the flow
of thoughtat the concept level; it is at the word-for-word, element-by-element
level that he does not understand. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Corinthians <b>15</b>:22
[universalism] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> As in Adam
all die, so all in Christ will be made alive. The passage does not say all are
in Adam or in Christ, but that all the ones in each group share the same
destiny as all the rest in either group, the same destiny as the representative
of the group.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Corinthians <b>15</b>:29-34
[baptism for the death]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>What
will they do who are baptized for the death?</i> Whatever Paul means, it
cannot be that he means one person can be saved in place of another. Guilt and
righteousness are personal; that is, guilt and righteousness are not
transferable from one person to another. The faith or obedience of one cannot
suffice for the obedience or salvation of another.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The best understanding
is that the apostle refers to [a] a <u>baptism <i>of suffering</i></u> (Mark <b>10</b>:38-39;
Luke <b>12</b>:50) [b] <u>on behalf of</u> [c] <u>the dead <i>in sin</i></u> (Ephesians
<b>2</b>:1, 5; Colossians <b>2</b>:13; 1 Timothy <b>5</b>:6). The next three
verses talk about significant suffering, not just suffering of any kind or suffering
for being Christians, but suffering incurred because of labor to save those who
are dead in sin. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>2 Corinthians<b> 9</b>:7 [God
loves a hilarious giver.] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Hilarious
has picked up a connotation in English that is not present in the Greek word it
translates (</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>). Giving is not
something lighthearted and frivolous. Cheerful or merciful catches a more
appropriate sense of the word. Cp. cheerfulness in Romans <b>12</b>:8.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Galatians <b>3</b>:28 [egalitarianism]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>In
Christ there is neither male nor female.</i> The dictum does not mean
everything that could conceivably be carried by its words; it refers to the
subject at hand: salvation. Salvation applies to all human persons regardless of
non-interpersonal differences among themnationality (Jew-Gentile), sex
(male-female), social status (slave-free), and the like. Paul speaks
soteriologically and behaviorally. The phrase cannot be removed from that
setting and put in some other context [homosexuality, equalitarianism] that
shapes its meaning in a way contradictory to Pauls teaching elsewhere.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See also passages
about female deference/submission.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Galatians <b>5</b>:4 [apostasy]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>You are
separated from Christ; you are fallen from grace.</i> It is a stretch to use
this text to argue for the possibility of apostasy. Pauls point is that
receiving circumcision in principle puts people under the Mosaic Lawa legal
system. In so doing, the Galatians would in theory be putting themselves out of
the grace systemthe Christ system. Law and grace-faith-promise are parallel, alternative
systems, not one within the other as if grace-faith (interpersonal) took place
inside of law (legal) or accomplished the purposes for the law which the law by
itself could not accomplish. Jewish Christians like James in Palestine were
still operating under the Law nationally speaking, but they were not therefore lost;
they were already Jews. Pauls circumcising Timothy made sense because he had a
Jewish mother (Acts <b>16</b>:1-3) and was therefore qualified to be a Jew
nationallyas in half-way covenant. Circumcising him would be for expediency to
remove an unnecessary hindrance in ministry among Jews. Circumcising Gentiles
had no such justification.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Ephesians <b>2</b>:1-5 [natural
depravity, original sin, original guilt]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Dead in
trespasses and sins</i> is not the writers precise wording. He says, <i>dead
through <u>your</u> trespasses and sins</i> and <i>among</i> [the disobedient]<i>
we once <u>lived</u> in the lusts of our flesh, <u>doing</u> the desires of the
flesh and mind.</i> The deadness comes <u>from our own actions</u>, not
Adams, and consists of <u>insensitivity to spiritual things</u>, not <u>insensibility</u>
to them, and of being separated from Christ (<b>2</b>:12; cp. dead to the
world), not from the living. Death <u>separates</u> from other persons and
makes people <u>insensitive</u> to spiritual (interpersonal) matters,
especially with God. Hardening (insensitivity, callousness) comes from the
deceitfulness of sin (Hebrews <b>3</b>:13), not from genetics. It is a
social, psychological term, not a physical, fleshly one.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Ephesians</span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
2</span></b><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:8-9
[faith as a gift]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>By
grace you have been saved through faith, and that not of yourselves; it is a gift
of God. </span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'>Paul does not have in mind the idea that a person
cannot believe on his own, that God has to enable him to believeto give
faith to him. <i>That</i> does not refer to <i>faith</i>, but to <i>salvation</i>,
the idea in the verb <i>saved.</i> <i>Faith</i> is feminine gender in the
Greek, but <i>that</i> is neuter. From the standpoint of grammatical concord
(agreement in number and gender between pronouns and antecedents), neuter <i>that</i>
cannot refer to the feminine word <i>faith</i>; it must refer to the abstract
idea in the verb <i>save</i>. Salvation is what is not of ourselves; that is a
gift from God. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>It
is worth noticing that when the apostles asked Jesus to increase their faith
(Luke <b>17</b>:5), he did not say, Here, have some more. He used the mustard
seed as an illustration. The implication is that we are to exercise the faith
we have, and in so doing it will increase to stronger faith when we see that it
works; so to speak, it will (increase to the point that is will) move
mountains. After all, the success of the faith lies, not in the strength of the
one exercising faith, but in the One they put faith in. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Ephesians<b> 4</b>:5 [trine
baptism; single mode of baptism]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>There
is one faith, one Lord, one baptism.</i> For some time, Paul has been talking
about the lack of Jew-Gentile distinction in the body of Christ. There are not
different Lords, faiths, baptisms, <i>etc</i>., for Jews on the one hand and
Gentiles on the other.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
passage should not be applied to the beliefs that (a) only one form of baptism
exists (rather than sprinkling, pouring, and immersion), that (b) threefold
baptism is not the case (a threefold application of water in the name of the
Father, and of the Son, and of the Holy Spirit each one), or (c) that there is
no baptism in the Holy Spirit because that occurred in the past only on
Pentecost (Jews) and at the household of Cornelius (Gentiles). Whatever
decision a person makes about these other matters must come from other
considerations besides the word one in this text.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Ephesians<b> 5</b>:18 [filled
with the Spirit]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Dont
get drunk on wine but be filled with the Spirit. </i>The text does not mean to
suggest that being filled with the Spirit is <u>like</u> being drunk. Paul
means the readers should <u>replace</u> being filled with spirits with being
filled with the Spirit. Filling is not a matter of achieving an altered state
of consciousness, a trance, or lapsing into uncontrolled behavior or ecstatic
utterance; it is the very opposite. The Spirit of God is a means of gaining self-control,
not of letting go. Paul is wording a <u>contrast</u>, not a <u>likeness</u>. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
idea that tongue speakers have no control over themselves is not a natural
inference from what the apostle says. He is not even talking about speaking in
tongues. He has a broader frame of reference in mind: Christian living. The
general rule Paul lays down is that <i>the spirits of the prophets are subject
to the prophets </i>(1 Corinthians <b>14</b>:32).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Ephesians </span><b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>5</span></b><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>:21
[mutual submission] [egalitarianism] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Submit
to one another</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> is taken to mean that husbands are to
submit to their wives in the same sense that wives are to submit to their
husbands. The husband is no more the head of the wife than the wife is head of
the husband. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>If
that were the point, one wonders what Paul means two verses later (<b>5</b>:23)
when he says that the husband is <i><u>head</u> of the wife like Christ is <u>head</u>
of the church.</i> Husbands are never said to submit to their wives, and wives
are never said to be the heads of their husbands.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>First,
the wording about submission applies to the whole section of household
teaching (<b>5</b>:22-6:9), not just the first part of itthe husband-wife part.
Ordering the whole household is what the apostle summarizes under the
admonition to <i>practice appropriate deference one to another</i>:
husband-wife, parent-children, and master-slave. Similar teaching occurs
elsewhere in Colossians <b>3</b>:18-4:1 and 1 Peter <b>3</b>:1-7. Noting that <b>5</b>:21
heads the whole section on household teaching clarifies the writers point
because father-children relationship shows what he means about husband-wife
relationship. Whatever may be said about parents submitting to children, does
not remove parental priority over them and responsibility for them. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Secondly,
the mistaken interpretation evidently comes also from supposing that the
reciprocal pronoun one another (</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>)
always means a-to-b-to-a. But one another applies as well to a-to-b plus c-to-d
within a group (one to another). The most obvious example is Revelation <b>6</b>:4
(killing one another). Other cases are Luke <b>7</b>:32 (<i>children calling
to one another</i>); Mathew <b>24</b>:10 (delivering one another up); people
in a crowd asking one another (Luke <b>8</b>:25 = Mark <b>4</b>:41; Mark <b>8</b>:16;
<b>15</b>:31; Luke <b>2</b>:15; <b>4</b>:36;<b> 6</b>:11; <b>8</b>:15; <b>20</b>:14;
<b>24</b>:32; John <b>4</b>:33; <b>5</b>:44; <b>6</b>:52; <b>11</b>:56; <b>16</b>:17;
<b>19</b>:24; Acts<b> 4</b>:15-16; <b>26</b>:31; <b>28</b>:14). See also Galatians
<b>6</b>:15 (<i>devouring one another </i>[?]); Luke <b>12</b>:1 (<i>treading
on one another </i>[?)]; Acts <b>7</b>:27 (<i>Why do you wrong one another? </i>[?]);
James <b>5</b>:16 (<i>Confess your sins to one another </i>[?]); Galatians <b>5</b>:13
(<i>Serve one another </i>[?]).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Thirdly,
husbands are never told to submit to their wives. Other teaching about the
household unit and husband-wife relations in Colossians, 1 Peter, and elsewhere
affords no opportunity for injecting the idea of husbands submitting to wives. The
general female deference principleregardless of how that strikes a modern western-world
readerpredicts that the New Testament will not contain a statement about
mutual submission in the sense advocated in the misreading. A writer must
always be read on his own terms, not those of the reader.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Fourthly,
proper understanding differentiates structure and personal relationship. Pauls
teaching in Ephesians<b> 5</b>:21-33 pertains to the <u>structure</u> of the
family unitand the natural behavioral implications of that arrangement. Order assigns
responsibility, the primary factor in leadership, which is then reinforced by
authority. It has nothing to do with greater worth, personal importance or
value, or greater ability. Being in charge is not a big deal; being in
submission is not a put down. Headship means having responsibility; being in
submission means acting in accordance with the other persons responsibility. Even
the creator of the universe was not made less by being subject to his parents
(Luke <b>2</b>:51). Submission, then, enjoins deference behavior so as not to
interfere with the persons responsibility. Besides the natural behaviors implied,
cultures may establish additional customs that signal compliance with the established
orderveil wearing, manner of verbal interaction, role fulfillments, dress, and
so on. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>In
the <u>interpersonal</u> dimension, of course, there is admonition to mutual considerateness
in sexual relations (1 Corinthians <b>7</b>:3-5), physical constitution (1
Peter <b>3</b>:7), and general interdependency (1 Corinthians <b>11</b>:8-12. In
fact, as regards gathered worship, comments about interdependency in 1
Corinthians <b>12</b>:8-12 appear in the very midst of instructions about
wives deference (modesty) behavior (<b>11</b>:2-7, 13-16). Obviously, Paul
distinguishes two things while relating them, one involving order and another involving
interdependency. The former sets the framework for its cultural ramification in
gathered worshipas between God and Christ and between Christ and a man and
between a man and wifeand the subsequent interpersonal operation between parties
at each level of the ordered relationship. The apostle uses the Christ-man
model to set the framework for his instructions about female worship demeanor. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>As
in church (1 Corinthians <b>11</b>), so in the home (Ephesians <b>5-6</b>). In
the very context about domestic order, Ephesians<b> 5</b>:21-6:9 commands
interpersonal actions expected within the structure. Husbands should love their
wives and give themselves for them as Christ did for the church. Fathers are
not to exasperate their children but nurture them in the admonition of the
Lord. Masters should act toward slaves as slaves should act toward masters,
forbearing threats. Personal behavior takes place within structured order in a
way that does not vitiate that interpersonal framework. The two dimensions do
not conflict because they deal with different matters, one with responsibility,
the other with interpersonal associations within it. They no more conflict than
the Sons love for the church conflicts with his leadership for the church
(head). In fact, in the Ephesians household teaching, there is more about the
modelChrists relationship to the churchthan there is about its domestic applications
of the model: <i>I am talking about Christ and the church</i> (<b>5</b>:32).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See Greek
Elements in the Interpretation of Ephesians <b>5</b>:21.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Ephesians <b>6</b>:17 [The
Holy Spirit operates only through the word.] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>The
sword of the Spirit, which is the word of God</i> is taken to mean that the
Holy Spirit operates only through the word, which is his sword. The message
in the Bible is the only means necessary for conviction and conversion. The
idea in the interpretation is meant to contrast with the belief that the Spirit
operates supernaturally parallel to the word to bring about the faith
(regeneration) and the Christian growth (sanctification) that takes place in
each person. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Whatever
a person decides about that idea, this text is at best not a clear statement of
it. The antecedent is more likely the <u>sword</u>, not the Spirit. The
antecedent of <i>which</i> (</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>)
is not apt to be <i>Spirit</i> though indeed which is neuter like <i>Spirit</i>
(</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:
115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>). However, the gender of a pronoun
subject (which) in a linking-verb clause sometimes attracts to the
predicate nominative (Gods word, </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>).
The conceptual antecedentthe phrase head in that caseis <i>sword</i> (</span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>,
feminine). </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Revelation
elsewhere indicates that the Spirit replaces the ascended Christ as another
Comforter (John<b> 14</b>:16-18, 26; <b>15</b>:26-27; <b>16</b>:7-15). That
promise involves a personal presence constantly available for guidance, power,
and intercession in an unknowlable degree through circumstance, scripture, and
other Christians. While some of these Comforter promises to the apostles may
not transfer to everyone after them, a plethora of texts about his work pertains
to this matter. At any rate, Ephesians <b>6</b>:17 should not be interpreted to
the point of de-activating and depersonalizing the Spirit in the Christian
life. The idea that supernatural operation is necessary for fruitful hearing of
the message is better handled from other considerations and other texts. If the
idea cannot be sustained from elsewhere, it is not something that has to be,
supposedly, disproved by an unnecessary take on this verse.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>See
also Romans <b>8</b>:26. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Philippians <b>1</b>:29 [faith
given]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>It
has been granted to you not only to believe, but to suffer on his behalf. </span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
expression give faith conjures up in the English readers mind a direct act
in which the receiver passively receives. The Hebrew background helps because
the same word for give also covers allow (</span><span lang=HE dir=RTL
style='font-size:14.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
dir=LTR></span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'><span
dir=LTR></span>).</span><span dir=RTL></span><span dir=RTL style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'><span dir=RTL></span>
</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Aside
from that consideration, it should be evident that the believing is no more a
direct deposit by God than is the suffering that is paired with it. Both are
circumstantial; having the opportunity to believe and being allowed to incur
suffering both come from the God who oversees all historical processes. Acts <b>11</b>:18
says similarly that to Gentiles <i>God has granted repentance to life,</i> meaning,
we take it, that the message, which highlights repentance, has been made
available to Gentiles to accept and profit from just as is has for Jews.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Philippians<b> 2</b>:10 [triple-deck
cosmos]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>In
the name of Jesus, every knee should bow, in heaven and on earth and under the
earth.</i> This text along with several others is taken to imply that the
writers thought of the cosmos as flat and perhaps in two or three layers: Luke <b>10</b>:15,
18;<b> 24</b>:51; Acts <b>1</b>:9-11 (ascent to heaven, descent to hell). The
Old Testament is full of references to the abode of the dead as being down. That
is not necessarily anything more than an extension of burying people down in
the ground. Revelation <b>7</b>:1; <b>20</b>:8 refers to four corners of the
earth. Ezekiel <b>7</b>:2 speaks of four corners of the land when the land did
not exactly have four corners (cp. Isaiah <b>11</b>:12). Ezekiel talks about
four winds (<b>37</b>:9) just like we talk today about four directions for
simplification purposes, meaning in every direction.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> It
seems satisfactory to regard such expressions as phenomenological language,
simplified language that comes from the way things look. People regularly talk
about the sun coming up and going down. People that have a modern cosmological
understanding still speak of heaven as up. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> When
a speakers subject is something else, listeners do well not to make too much
of how he describes the off-subject. If he is deliberately giving a description
of the universe, he might not use such loose language.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Philippians <b>2</b>:12 [works]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Work out
your salvation with fear and trembling</i> is a comment Paul is making about
living out the implications of salvation or the way they live. He is not
talking about <u>obtaining</u> salvation but with maintaining and improving it.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Philippians <b>2</b>:13 [the
work of the Spirit in the believer]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>God
works in you both to will and to work for his good pleasure.</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
Some interpreters give this text a supernatural reading according to which the
Spirit energizes the human will and mind to enable what would not be possible or
particularly satisfactory without such enablement. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
issue is not <u>what</u> the Spirit does in and to the inner man, but <u>how</u>
he assists a believer, Is it by personal influence or supernatural power? It is
sufficient to understand that he works directly by the influence of sheer
presence and indirectly through providential circumstance, the word, and other
people who have appropriated the word and redistribute the Spirits influence
interpersonally to others. Without supernatural operation already in mind along
with the doctrine of natural depravity that it supposedly remedies in this
process, such a hypostatic take on the text would not commend itself or even
come into the picture. In place of <u>supernatural</u> activity directly on a
persons inner being (hypostatically/substantively/onticly), we suppose Paul
means a normal <u>interpersonal</u> kind of activitywithout denying the
possibility of something more that is not uniform.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Colossians <b>1</b>:15 [createdness
of the Son] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
Son of his love . . . who is the firstborn of all creation,</span></i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> taken
to mean that the Son was created first (first-<i>born</i>), and then he created
everything else. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Firstborn
has another, different intent of meaning. According to ancient Jewish custom,
the firstborn son in a family was given the responsibility for the ongoing
welfare of the family (primogeniture). He received a double portion of the
inheritance in payment for accomplishing that role. While it is true that
physical sons had a beginning in this situation, the dominion Christ has over
the creation satisfies the reason for labeling him firstborn (primogenitor).
Paul is not talking about a literal firstborn over a family; hence, the figurative
nature of his comment should be clear. The figurative meaning needs go no
further than the dominion and concomitant responsibility he has as Son of
God. Otherwise, the interpretation allegorizes the comparison. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Without
some clear statement anywhere else regarding the createdness of the Son, there
is not much reason to suppose he is a created being, therefore not eternal from
the past or deity in nature. Is <i>firstborn</i> meant in the sense of position
or time? The approach here takes it as position. At least, the unclear should
be taken in light of the clear. A passage that could conceivably be understood
in more than one way, cannot serve as a basis for an idea not witnessed
elsewhere.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
trinity issue is really what lies behind the urge to press this text into meaning
more than it has to mean. Choosing an interpretation for this passage will not
happen until readers have settled the trinity question in their own mind. Many
texts like this one are not in themselves capable of producing a definite
meaning because a persons general framework of thought establishes a context
that sorts through possible meanings to determine intended meaning.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Colossians<b>
1</b>:18; Revelation <b>3</b>:14; <b>21</b>:6 can be brought in as part of this
same discussion of the word <i>beginning</i>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Thessalonians <b>5</b>:17
[ceaseless prayer]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Pray
without ceasing</span></i><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> taken as continuous action presents a
difficulty for the practical significance Pauls wording. A person cannot pray
endlessly; so interpreters undertake to find other applications of the
apostles words. One tack is to make the verse mean that a person is in a
constant attitude of prayer. A similar one redefines pray into a label for
the upward directedness of life.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>To
avoid such unlikely values of the command, readers need to shift from
continuous action to characteristic or iterative action. In that vein, the
command becomes practical: Christians need to maintain a healthy practice of
praying, not continuously, of course, but characteristically. Praying is a
recurring action directed toward God. There are gaps in the actual act
(iterative), but worshippers maintain a wholesome pattern of relating to God in
this way.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Thessalonians <b>5</b>:18
[gratitude] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i><u>In</u>
everything give thanks</i> does not mean the same thing as <i>give thanks <u>for</u>
everything,</i> even though a person could probably make practical sense out
of the second wording. Most everything, good or bad, has a silver lining,
teaches us something we could not learn otherwise, strengthens us in ways we
would not have grown into without the adversity, provides an opportunity to
glorify God in a fashion that lesser trials would not have produced.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> A more
practical understanding of the admonition says that, regardless of the
circumstances, we still find things to be thankful for. Paramount among them is
a basis for having a positive attitude toward life and the future despite the
distracting aggravations that come on us. Pauls advice highlights lifes
positives to counteract the defeatists tendency to dwell on negatives. It
avoids the unrealistic yearning for a better situation that will probably never
come.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Thessalonians<b> 5</b>:19
[advocating special gifts]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Dont
quench the Spirit; dont disregard prophesyings.</i> Those zealous for modern
supernatural gifts take the apostles admonitions as a response to others who
question whether what are happening are in fact special manifestations of the
Spirit and of prophesying. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Without
settling first whether cases of language speaking, prophesying, interpretation,
or special healing, for instance, are what the New Testament is talking about,
the passage here does little to remove being careful about claimed cases of
supernatural manifestation. The New Testament languages speaking was talking
in unstudied, foreign, real languages of the day. New Testament examples of
healing were instantaneous and uniformly successful. If examples urged for
acceptance do not measure up to those definitions, setting them aside as miracles
does not come from an unwillingness to accept current supernatural manifestation.
It comes from not wanting to regard them erroneously as genuine and then be led
into false teaching, false expectancies, or undue regard for the miracle workers
or even miraculous manifestations themselves. In scripture, authenticating the
claims of the miracle worker was one of the purposes of miracle. Defining the
gifts must precede exhorting the use of them or acceptance of them.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
same pattern of thought applies to evaluating prophecies, revelations,
prophesyings, interpretations, healings, and the like. If they are, in fact,
instances of people speaking presumptuously (Deuteronomy <b>18</b>:20-22), we
should not get caught up in the ideas, predictions, and exhortations they accompany.
Willingness to <i>test the spirits</i> (1 John <b>4</b>:1-3) is as much a
part of Christian faithfulness as willingness to be led by the Spirit. Testing
the spirits applies to <u>distinguishing genuine miracle from <i>lying wonders</i></u>
(Matthew <b>24</b>:24 = Mark <b>13</b>:22). The testing involves applying the
doctrinal test (cp. Deuteronomy <b>13</b>:1-5; <b>18</b>:20-22; Galatians <b>1</b>:8-9).
Using 1 Thessalonians <b>5</b>:19-20 to lower resistance to false doctrine and
practice works against the Spirits real concern in the world today. That real
concern is not so much displaying supernatural phenomena as it is in properly
leading people into proper relationship to God through Christ.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Testing
the spirits also applies to <u>distinguishing miracle from answered prayer</u>.
Everything involved in one is available through the other. The difference lies
in the lack of uniform success, immediate results, and the availability of
answered prayer to all Christs followers, not just specially gifted ones. The
distinguishing elements in miracle relate to their <u>apologetic purpose</u>: uniform
success, immediate and permanent results; without such marks, evidential value
would be lost. Answered prayer is more for benefit than for proof.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Testing
could also apply to cases where the one doing the signs thinks he is doing
something supernatural when he is not. It might be answered prayer instead, or
some psychological effect, </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> 1
Thessalonians<b> 5</b>:19 also warrants considering a related matter. Some
uncertainty exists about whether <i>spirit</i> should be capitalized here. The Greek
and Hebrew original does not show when to capitalize the usual English translation
of </span><span lang=HE dir=RTL style='font-size:14.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span dir=LTR></span><span
lang=HE style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'><span
dir=LTR></span> </span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;
font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>and </span><span lang=EL style='font-size:
12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>
</span><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>as
<i>S/spirit</i>, that is, whether a given usage is proper name or a noun
description. Each time the word occurs in either testament, the reader must
make that determination. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Paul
may not be speaking here about the Holy Spirit particularly. His exhortation can
make good sense when taken to mean (a) the spirit/atmosphere/morale among the
believers, which could (b) hinder the Spirits work by improper attitude,
motives, and behavior. Likewise, prophesying does not necessarily involve
special giftedness either since it is broader than predicting the future; it
may stress more basically a persons role in speaking on behalf of another. Straining
after miracle may not signify the Christian maturity that the first comments
above may illustrate. Christianity is not so much about having supernatural experiences
as it is about properly functioning in the natural, interpersonal realm God
created for us from the beginning.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See Tongues
in Corinth and other essays in apologetics, in Holy Spirit studies, and in Greek
helps <i>in loco</i>.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Timothy <b>3</b>:2, 12 [married
elders and deacons]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Husband
of one wife means to some that elders and deacons must be married men. Such a
take provides a ready way of responding to the requirement for a celibate
clergy. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>While
such a meaning is conceivable from the words aside from contextual
considerations, that cannot be the writers point. He begins his list of
qualities in 1 Timothy <b>3</b>:2 by using the general word without reproach,
immediately after which he says husband of one wife and remarks again about
the need to be without reproach. Then he lists a series of behavioral
maturities that should be evident in a leader. Being unmarried is not a matter
of reproach or Paul himself would not be without reproach (1 Corinthians <b>9</b>:5)or
Jesus himself, for that matter. Furthermore, the Corinthians would come under
reproach if they had taken his advice not to marry lest it distract from
ministering in these offices (1 Corinthians <b>7</b>:32-35). See especially
Titus <b>1</b>:6 + 7.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>In
keeping with the emphasis on qualities, the Greek text does not carry an
article on husband. That absence implies quality <i>vs.</i> identity, which
the article would convey. The force of the comment is a one-woman kind of
man.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>In
regard to deacons (<b>3</b>:8-10, 12-13), one section of qualifications ends
with <i>let them serve . . . if they are blameless</i> (<b>3</b>:10). In
laying out these characteristics, Pauls concern is with personal character,
not legal status. That verdict comes even more clearly in Titus <b>1</b>:6,
where Paul brackets <i>husband of one wife</i> with the word blameless: <i><u>for</u>
a bishop must be blameless.</i> A similar qualification for church support of
widows includes the item wife of one husband (1 Timothy <b>5</b>:9). Being
married and having a plurality of children is not a matter of blamelessness.
Marital statuses that are not moral matters lie beyond the authors purview
(celibacy, deuterogamy, legitimately divorced and remarried). The expression marks
a moral qualification, not a social status.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> See also
Titus <b>1</b>:5-9; 1 Timothy <b>5</b>:9.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Timothy <b>2</b>:15 [saved
by childbearing]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Salvation is
a spiritual matter, not a physical one. The force of the characteristic is more
child-rearing since physical abilities do not accomplish spiritual purposes.
There is a general correlation between the quality of parents and the quality
of their offspring. Eschewing familial responsibilities does not serve well the
stable nature established by families rather than individuals.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Timothy<b> 6</b>:10 [wealth]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The text is
frequently misquoted to say, <i><u>Money</u> is the root of all evil,</i>
whereas it says, <i>The <u>love of money</u> is the root of all evils.</i>
The point is not that wealth in itself is evil or that wealthy people cannot be
Christians. Lydia was a seller of purple (Acts <b>16</b>:14), presumably a
lucrative business. The point also is not that evil can always be traced back
to money. All can better be taken in the sense of all kinds of evil or as
a significant general cause of evil.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Hebrews <b>7</b>:8 [Christian
tithing]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Here
men that die receive tithes [Levites], but there one of whom it is witnessed
that he lives </i>[Melchizedek]<i>. </i>The Hebrew writer is contrasting
Levites with Melchizedek, whom the author has just said was <i>without
beginning of days or end of life</i> (<b>7</b>:3). To serve as a basis for
advocating tithing, some have taken he lives as a reference to Christ. The
context, however, is showing the superiority of Melchidezek to Abraham; Christ
is not part of the immediate comments. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Christ
