<u>compares</u> with Melchizedek only in that Melchizedek was a priest of God
Most High, as Psalm <b>110</b>:4 says (Hebrews <b>5</b>:6-<b>7</b>:25). The
Hebrew writer emphasizes the <u>contrasts</u> between Melchizedek and Levispecifically
that the Melchizedekian priest was appointed with an <u>oath</u> (Hebrews <b>7</b>:21),
was appointed after, was appointed <u>forever</u> (Hebrews <b>7</b>:17), and
was <u>distinct</u> from the Levitical system (<b>7</b>:6b). So his is a <u>better</u>
priesthood that <u>replaces</u> the Levitical priesthood. Being comparable to
Christ in being a priest (office) does not mean he was so in every (unspecified)
respectas in receiving tithes (functioning of the office). Besides, as far as
the record goes, Abrahams tithe to Melchizedek was a one-time offering from
the spoils of war (Hebrews <b>7</b>:1) rather than an ongoing practice from
personal possessions.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Hebrews <b>7</b>:14 [prohibition
of instrumental music] [silence as prohibition in worship matters]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Concerning
which tribe Moses spoke nothing about priests</i> comments on who has a right
to serve as a priest. The writer is not talking about <u>operating</u> in an
officeas in worship, but about qualifications for <u>holding</u> an office.
Priests led in worship, but the topic is who had a right to conduct that worship.
The subject is the <u>office</u> of priest (priesthood), not the <u>activities</u>
of a priest (worship). Silence amounts to prohibition in matters of office
qualifications; silence about persons with other characteristics eliminates
other candidates from holding the office. The verse does not serve, then, as a
basis for the proposition that silence in matters of worship amounts to
prohibition and that since mechanical instruments are not commanded in the New
Testament, there is no justification for their use in worship.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> As with so
many other topics in misused texts, whatever person thinks about the
non-instrument position, this passage is no basis for it. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Hebrews <b>11</b>:7 [no
rain before the flood.]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Hebrews <b>13</b>:8 [immutability
of Christ] [permanence of miraculous manifestation]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Jesus is
the same yesterday, today, and forever.</i> Advocates of language speaking today
argue from this verse that glossolalia is a permanent feature of the church
age. To the contrary, the statement is that <u>Jesus</u> is the same
(unchangeableness), not that he does things the same way permanently. Besides, languages
would be a feature of the afterlife as well since the verse ends, <i>Jesus is
the same . . . <u>forever</u>.</i> Nevertheless, Paul says that languages are
not part of the perfect; they will cease; so they are <u>not</u> forever (1
Corinthians <b>13</b>:8). Finally, if Christ always operates the same way, how
would the Pentecostal outpouring (Acts<b> 2</b>) fit with bringing in such
manifestations as were not present before?</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>James <b>2</b>:10 [sin in
one thing, (may as well be) guilty of all]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Whoever
keeps the whole law and yet stumbles in one [matter] has become guilty of all.
</i>The point is not that in doing one thing wrong, a person may as well have
broken all the laws. Rather, it means that whoever breaks one ordinance in the Law
has become guilty of breaking the Law as a wholeguilty of lawbreaking; a
person is a lawbreaker and no longer perfect. Perfection is the only standard
under law (Deuteronomy <b>27</b>:26). A person is still a lawbreaker whether by
little or by much. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
passage also does not mean that all sins are equally bad. All sins are equal in
being sins; but as respects consequences, all sins are <u>not equally
destructive</u>, and all sins are <u>not punished equally</u>some with many
stripes, others with few (Luke<b> 2</b>:48).</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>James<b> 2</b>:10 [One
sin is as bad as another.]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> All sins are
alike in being sins, but they are not alike in how much damage they do or in
the degree of bad attitude they issue out of.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>James <b>5</b>:12 [oath
taking; see above at Matthew <b>5</b>:33-37]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>James <b>5</b>:16 [clerical
absolution, the confessional]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Confess
your sins to one another.</i> Clerical absolution puts this admonition in the
service of the confessional and the clerical forgiveness of sins. The
reciprocal pronoun here is a-to-b-to-a, as shown by the parallelism with pray
for one another. Without supplying a legal framework, such wording cannot have
the one-directional value that the practice of the confessional assumes. The
authority of a legal framework has to come into the picture to make something interpersonally
artificial into something legitimate. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Confessing
sins to one another is a different thing from confessing sins to a third party
to get his forgiveness for what was done to someone else. We confess sins to
one another (a) if the other is the second party, the one sinned against; (b)
if the other is a third party who serves as an intermediary for communication
purposes; (c) if the other person is a third party we can call on for advice on
how to handle the situation; and (d) for strengthening purposes. The general
principle in confession among people is <i>I to you if you to me.</i></span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1 Peter <b>3</b>:3 [braided
hair and wearing jewelry]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Let not
your adorning be with braided hair or wearing jewels of gold</i> is a partial
statement that sounds like Peter is prohibiting braided hair and jewelry. But
the verse goes on to add putting on clothes. Obviously, the apostle does not
mean women are to go naked. He is urging them to prioritize inner beauty over
outer appearance, and to rely on how they are within instead of how they look
on the outside. That is an entirely different thing from prohibiting looking
nice. Besides, it seems that current practice was like it can be in the modern
setting where extreme attention is given to ostentatious display in dress. The
situation also involves the Semitic tendency to speak of things in binary
fashion, one or the other, instead of by degrees.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>2 Peter <b>1</b>:4 [divinization]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> Peter
speaks of becoming partakers of the divine nature. Nature does not refer to
something substantive, but to the kind of persons behaviorally Christian work
toward becoming. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify;text-indent:.5in'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>The
goal of the Christian enterprise is not to change in substance, but to change
in personal relationship by changing behaviorrelationship to God and other
people. People do not become deity; they are not to become gods but more
godlike in conduct: <i>You shall be holy because I am holy </i>(Leviticus <b>11</b>:44;
1 Peter <b>1</b>:16). A hypostatic, or substantive/ontic, reading of such
language started happening when the Christian gospel hit the Gentile world. Greek
thought was steeped in ontic (stuff) issues as manifested in its many strands
of philosophy represented in Gnosticism, Manichaeism, Epicureanism, Stoicism,
Platonism, and so on. Hypostatic thought fudges scripture statements from
characteristic behavioral nature into substantive nature and thereby perverts
the concern of the faith and misses the benefits that come from peoples
concerning themselves with the correct category. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>1
John<b> 3</b>:6, 9; <b>5</b>:18 [sinlessness of the sanctified Christian] </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> <i>Whoever
is begotten by God does not sin.</i> They may make mistakes (non-moral
matters), but they will not sin. They can live a perfect life of love. A second
definite supernatural work of grace by the Spirit is then postulated to
eradicate the last vestiges of in-bred sin.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> But
1 John<b> 1</b>:9-12 is a control passages: <i>If we say we have no sins, we
deceive ourselves, we make him a liar, and the truth is not in us. </i>In the
first passage, John speaks of characteristic sin as something a Christian does
not do. John may be giving a warning against the kind of Gnostic teaching that
made moral behavior irrelevant. In Gnostic dualism, matter was considered unable
to affect spirit; so some gnostic teachers concluded that behavior in the flesh
(matter) was irrelevant. Salvation in that system was from admixture of
spirit and matter, not from alienation between persons.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><i><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></i></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Revelation <b>3</b>:14 [Christ
as the beginning of Gods creation]</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify'><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> The
verse gets caught up in the difficulty many Christians have with the trinity
issue. One proposal for solving that three-in-one issue is to make God (the
Father) the one referred to basically as God. This verse can then be brought
in to say that Christ was the first that God created (<i>beginning of Gods
creative activity</i>), and Christ perhaps turned around and created the rest
of reality. If Christ is considered deity under the God terminology, it can
be taken in a functional sense, that is, as carrying out the work of God. </span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>At
any rate, the discussion in this passage turns on the meaning of the word <i>beginning</i>
(</span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:
"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span lang=EL style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> </span><span lang=EL
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'></span><span
style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>).
Is it to be taken in the sense of time or status? It is the same question that
belongs to the word <i>firstborn</i> in Colossians <b>1</b>:15; so see the
comments herein on that text as well as part of the same discussion and as
turning on the same distinction between usages: time or status.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Colossians
<b>1</b>:18; Revelation<b> 3</b>:14; <b>21</b>:6 can be brought in as part of
this same discussion.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='margin-bottom:0in;text-align:justify;text-indent:
.5in'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>&nbsp;</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>Revelation<b> 7</b>:1 [The
earth is flat.] See also Isaiah <b>11</b>:12; Psalm <b>93</b>:1.</span></p>

<p class=MsoNormal style='text-align:justify'><span style='font-size:12.0pt;
line-height:115%;font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'> 
</span><span style='font-family:"Times New Roman",serif'>christir.org</span></p>
</div>
